Guarantee of a Magian's Mawqudha and a Dhimmi's Pig
Fatwa on liability for non-halal animal property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for destroying a Magian's mawqudha and a dhimmi's pig, and explain why the destroyer pays value rather than the like for a pig.

Core Idea
Non-Muslim property is protected even if the object is impure; destruction requires compensation.

This lesson examines a ruling from al-Tatarakhaniyya, a later Hanafi fatwa collection, which clarifies that a Muslim must guarantee the value of a Magian's animal killed by stunning (mawqudha). It then extends this principle to a dhimmi's pig, and contrasts it with the ruling for wine. Understanding this case deepens your grasp of how Hanafi fiqh protects the property rights of non-Muslims under Islamic jurisdiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary scenario
Focus on the case: a Muslim destroys a Magian's mawqudha (an animal killed by a blow, not slaughtered). The passage states the correct view (al-sahih) is that the Muslim guarantees it.
The Magian's animal, though not halal for Muslims, is still property.
2
Extend to the dhimmi's pig
The commentator had not addressed a dhimmi's pig; the passage analogizes it to the mawqudha case, ruling that the destroyer (Muslim or dhimmi) must pay its value.
The pig is considered like a sheep for a dhimmi in terms of property value.
3
Contrast with the wine rules
The passage restates: if a dhimmi destroys another dhimmi's wine, he pays the like (mithl); if a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine, he pays value (qimah). For a pig, both pay value because it has no like.
Wine is fungible (mithli), pig is non-fungible (qimi).
4
Note the authority chain
The ruling comes from al-Tatarakhaniyya, a later source. The author notes that al-Faqir initially could not find a transmission, then later found it, showing the importance of textual verification in fatwa.
This is fatawa mutakhkhirin, not from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : ولو أتلف موقوذة المجوسي مسلم الصحيح أنه يضمنها . ولم يتعرض الشارح لما يلزمه في إتلاف خنزير الذمي، والظاهر أنه يضمن قيمته كما لو كان شاة كما في موقوذة المجوسي أخذاً من قولهم كذلك الخنزير في حقهم كالشاة في حقنا والتفصيل المتقدم في الإسلام في خمر الذمي يجري في خنزيره وقد قال الفقير : هذا من غير أن يجد نقلاً ثم ظفرت بالنقل. وفي التتارخانية : وإن كان الخمر والخنزير لذمي يجب على متلفهما، سواء كان المتلف مسلماً أو ذمياً، غير أن المتلف إن كان ذمياً فإنه يجب عليه مثل الخمر، وإن كان المتلف مسلماً يجب عليه قيمة الخمر، وفي الخنزير يجب عليهما القيمة لأن الخنزير لا مثل له من جنسه.
And in al-Tatarakhaniyya: If a Muslim destroys a 'mawqudha' (animal killed by stunning) of a Magian, the correct view is that he guarantees it. And the commentator did not address what is required in the destruction of a dhimmi's pig. It is apparent that he guarantees its value, just as if it were a sheep, as in the case of the Magian's mawqudha, taking from their statement: 'Similarly, the pig is for them like the sheep for us.' And the detailed discussion earlier about conversion regarding the dhimmi's wine applies to his pig. And al-Faqir said: This is without finding a transmission, then I found the transmission. And in al-Tatarakhaniyya: If the wine and pig belong to a dhimmi, the destroyer must guarantee them, whether the destroyer is Muslim or dhimmi, except that if the destroyer is a dhimmi, he must pay the like of the wine, and if the destroyer is Muslim, he must pay the value of the wine. And for the pig, both must pay the value because the pig has no like of its kind.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage cites al-Tatarakhaniyya for the ruling that a Muslim must guarantee the value of a Magian's animal killed by stunning (mawqudha), even though such meat is not permissible for Muslims. It then extends this to the pig of a dhimmi, concluding that the value is guaranteed. There is a note that al-Faqir initially did not find a transmission but later found one. The text also restates earlier rulings on wine: a dhimmi destroyer pays the like; a Muslim destroyer pays the value. For pig, both pay value because it is non-fungible.

Key Terms
موقوذة (mawqudha): An animal killed by a blow, not by proper slaughter; still considered property for its owner.
مجوسي (majusi): Magian; a Zoroastrian, considered among the People of the Book in some respects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Tatarakhaniyya, what is the ruling if a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's pig?
A) No guarantee, because the pig is impure.
B) The Muslim must pay the value of the pig.
C) The Muslim must pay the like (another pig).
D) The Muslim must pay double the value because the pig is haram.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, non-Muslims' property is protected even if ritually impure; destruction of such property requires compensation by value (qimah) for non-fungible items like the pig, and by value or like (mithl) for fungible items like wine, depending on the destroyer's religion.

Guarantee for Defective Items and Ruined Matting
Ruling statements on guarantee for defective items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on guarantee when a defective item is destroyed and apply the condition of ability to restore to determine compensation.

Core Idea
No guarantee for destroying an item that had no value, but guarantee applies when valuable property cannot be restored.

This passage from al-Tatarakhaniyya clarifies several scenarios where the Hanafi school rules on guarantee (ḍamān). The key principle is that guarantee is only due when the item had legitimate value and cannot be restored. Rotten eggs, fake coins, and items that can be repaired are examined to illustrate this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and its condition
Determine what was destroyed and whether it was sound or defective (rotten, counterfeit, etc.).
2
Determine if the item had marketable value
If the item had no value at the time of destruction (e.g., rotten egg, fake coin), there is no guarantee.
Value is based on normal market conditions.
3
If the item had value, check if it can be restored
For items like a mat that can be repaired to original condition, the usurper is ordered to restore it, not pay money.
Restoration eliminates the need for monetary compensation.
4
If restoration is impossible, guarantee is required
If the item cannot be restored to its original state, the usurper must pay the value of the item in sound condition.
Analogous to a ladder with its rungs removed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتاخانية: أو كسر بيضة أو جوزة فوجد داخلها فاسداً فلا ضمان عليه كذا لو كسر دراهم إنسان ثم ظهر أنها ستوقة فلا ضمان عليه، وإذا أفسد تأليف حصر إنسان فإن أمكن اعادته كما كان أمرناه بها فصار كما لو غصب سلم إنسان وفرق سياهها وإن لم يمكن الإعادة كما كان سلم المنقوض سياهاً وضمن قيمة الحصر صحيحاً.
And in al-Tatarakhaniyya: If he breaks an egg or a walnut and finds its inside rotten, there is no guarantee. Similarly, if he breaks someone's dirhams and then it appears they were counterfeit, there is no guarantee. And if he ruins the composition of someone's mat, then if it can be restored as it was, we order him to do so, so it becomes like when he usurped a person's ladder and separated its rungs. And if it cannot be restored as it was, like an upturned ladder with its rungs removed, he guarantees the value of the mat in sound condition.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage gives several rulings on guarantee when the item destroyed is defective. If someone breaks an egg or walnut and discovers it is rotten inside, no guarantee because it had no value. Similarly, if he breaks dirhams that turn out to be counterfeit, no guarantee. For a mat whose composition is ruined, if it can be restored to its original condition, the usurper is ordered to restore it; if not, he must pay the value of the mat in sound condition. This is analogous to usurping a ladder and separating its rungs.

Key Terms
ستوقة (sutuqa): Counterfeit coins; fake money.
حصر (hasr): A mat; woven flooring material.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone breaks another person's mat and it can be restored to its original condition?
A) He must pay the value of the mat in sound condition.
B) He is ordered to restore it to its original condition.
C) He is not liable because the mat was defective.
D) He must pay a fine.

Key Takeaway
Guarantee in Hanafi law applies only to items that had value and cannot be restored; for valueless or restorable items, no monetary compensation is due.

Wine as Property for Dhimmis
Establishing property status of wine for dhimmis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that wine is muttaqawwim for dhimmis and explain the evidence from the Prophet's practice and consensus.

Core Idea
Wine is legally valuable property for dhimmis by Prophetic instruction and consensus.

This lesson establishes the fundamental Hanafi ruling that wine is considered property (muttaqawwim) for dhimmis, enabling them to sell it and requiring compensation if destroyed. The evidence comes from the Prophet's instruction to collect tax from the sale of their wine, which would be meaningless if wine had no legal value. We will examine the proof and address an objection raised against it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key evidence
Find the hadith cited: the Prophet told tax collectors not to take tax directly from the wine but to let dhimmis sell it and then take a tenth of the proceeds. This implies the wine is property and sale is valid.
The hadith is a sound practice (zanni al-thubut).
2
Understand the consensus argument
The passage states that 'were it not considered property, he would not have ordered them to do so without objection' and calls it a consensus (ijma). Recognize that this is a basis for the rule.
Consensus is a strong evidence in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the objection from Al-Inayah
The objection compares wine to other prohibited acts (building churches, riding horses) that dhimmis are prevented from doing. Note how the passage distinguishes wine by the specific permission of the Prophet.
The key distinction: religious prohibition applies to Muslims, not dhimmis, unless explicitly stated.
4
Evaluate the exceptions
The passage contrasts carrion and blood (never property), riba (excepted by hadith), apostate slave (transferred), and meat without tasmiyah (ruling differs). Understand why wine is treated differently.
These exceptions reinforce that wine is unique in being property for dhimmis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخمر، فقالوا: نعشرها . قال : لا تفعلوا وولوهم بيعها وخذوا العشر من أثمانها. فلولا أنها متقومة وبيعها جائز لهم لما أمرهم بذلك من غير إنكار فكان إجماعاً. وأورد على هذا الدليل في العناية فقال : لم لا تتركهم وما يدينون في بعض الأمور كإحداث بيعة وكنيسة وكركوب الخيل وحمل السلاح فإنهم يمنعون منها، ولأن الأمر باجتناب الرجس يتناول المسلم فبقي في حق الكافر على ما كان بخلاف الميتة والدم لأن أحداً لا يعتقد تقومهما وبخلاف الربا فإنه مستثنى من عقودهم لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا من أربي فليس بيننا وبينه عهد» وبخلاف العبد المرتد يكون للذمي فإنا نقلته لأنا ما ضمنا لهم ترك التعرض لما فيه من الاستحقاق بالدين وبخلاف متروك التسمية عمداً فإذا كان الذابح من المسلمين لأن ولاية السيف والمحاجة ثابتة فيمكن إلزامه فلا يجب على متلفه الضمان وأما إذا أتلف المسلم خمر الذمي تجب عليه قيمته وإن كان مثلياً لأن المسلم ممنوع من تملكه وتمليكه بخلاف الذمي إذا استهلك خمر الذمي حيث يجب عليه مثله لقدرته عليه، ولو أسلم الطالب بعد ما قضى عليه بمثلها فلا شيء له على المطلوب لأن الخمر في حقه ليس بمتقوم فكان بإسلامه مبرأ عما كان في ذمته من وكذا لو أسلما لأن في إسلامهما إسلام الطالب . ولو أسلم المطلوب ثم أسلم الطالب بعده قال أبو يوسف : لا يجب عليه شيء. وقال محمد : يجب عليه قيمة الخمر وهي رواية عن الإمام لأن الإسلام الطارئ بعد تقرر السبب كالإسلام المقارن للسبب وهو لا يمنع وجوب قيمة الخمر على المسلم، ولأبي يوسف أن قبض الخمر المستحق في الذمة فقد تعذر استيفاؤه بسبب الإسلام ولا يمكن إيجاب قيمتها أيضاً لأنه ممنوع منها وصار كما لو كسر قلباً لغيره ثم تلف المكسور في يد صاحبه ليس لصاحبه أن يضمن الكاسر شيئاً لأن شرط تضمين قيمته تمليك المكسور وذلك قد فات ودليله مذكور في المطولات.
Regarding wine, they said: We take a tenth of it. He (the Prophet) said: Do not do so, but rather entrust them with its sale and take a tenth from its price. Were it not considered property and its sale permissible for them, he would not have ordered them to do so without objection, and it was thus a consensus. An objection was raised to this evidence in Al-Inayah: Why not leave them to their religion in some matters, like building a church or synagogue, riding horses, or carrying weapons, for which they are prevented? And because the command to avoid filth applies to Muslims, so it remains for the disbeliever as it was, unlike carrion and blood because no one believes them to be property, and unlike riba which is excepted from their contracts due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Except the one who deals in riba, for there is no covenant between us and him,' and unlike an apostate slave belonging to a dhimmi, for we transfer it because we did not guarantee to refrain from interfering with what is due by religion, and unlike the case of intentionally omitting the tasmiyah when the slaughterer is Muslim, because the authority of the sword and argument is established and can be enforced, so no compensation is due for its destruction. As for when a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine, its value is obligatory upon him even if it is a fungible, because the Muslim is prohibited from owning or transferring it, unlike when a dhimmi consumes another dhimmi's wine, where the like is obligatory because he is capable of it. If the claimant (plaintiff) converts to Islam after judgment for the like (wine), there is nothing for him against the defendant, because wine is not property for him, and by his Islam he is absolved from what was in his liability. Similarly if both convert, because their conversion is the conversion of the claimant. If the defendant converts first and then the claimant converts later, Abu Yusuf said: Nothing is obligatory upon him. Muhammad said: The value of the wine is obligatory, and this is a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), because Islam arising after the cause is established is like Islam concurrent with the cause, and it does not prevent the obligation of the value of the wine upon the Muslim. According to Abu Yusuf, the wine was due in the liability, but its recovery became impossible due to Islam, and its value cannot be made obligatory either because he is prohibited from it, and it is like the case of someone who breaks another's pot and then the broken pot is lost in the owner's hand; the owner cannot make the breaker liable for anything because the condition for guaranteeing its value is the transfer of ownership of the broken object, and that is no longer possible. Its evidence is mentioned in the extended works.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage establishes that wine is considered property (muttaqawwim) for dhimmis, based on the Prophet's instruction to let them sell their wine and take jizya from the proceeds. The ruling is supported by consensus (ijma). The commentator then addresses an objection from Al-Inayah comparing wine to other prohibited acts. The key point is that the Quranic prohibition of wine applies only to Muslims, so for dhimmis it remains permissible and valuable. The passage then discusses the guarantee when a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine. Notably, if the dhimmi later converts to Islam, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the guarantee drops because the wine is no longer property for the claimant, while Muhammad (and a narration from Abu Hanifa) holds that the value is still due because the liability was established before conversion. The analogy of a broken pot clarifies Abu Yusuf's reasoning. Students should note the principle that the time of the act determines liability, and that wine in the hands of a Muslim is not property but in the hands of a dhimmi it is.

Key Terms
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and protected, so destruction requires compensation.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject whose property rights are recognized for certain items.
قيمة (qīmah): Market value, used when the like (mithl) is not possible due to prohibition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence that wine is property for dhimmis?
A) Analogy to other permissible contracts
B) The Prophet's instruction to let them sell wine and tax the proceeds
C) The consensus of the four schools
D) The absence of a Quranic prohibition for dhimmis

Key Takeaway
Wine is legally valuable property for dhimmis because the Prophet explicitly permitted its sale, establishing a consensus.

6 min
Guarantee When Muslim Destroys Dhimmi's Wine
Ruling on Muslim's guarantee of dhimmi's wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a Muslim must guarantee the value (qimah) of a dhimmi's wine, and distinguish between qimah and mithl.

Core Idea
A Muslim who destroys a dhimmi's wine must pay its value because the Muslim cannot own or transfer wine.

This lesson examines the direct ruling when a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine. The Hanafi school holds that the Muslim must pay the market value (qimah), not the like (mithl), because the Muslim is prohibited from owning or transferring wine. We contrast this with the case of a dhimmi who destroys another dhimmi's wine, where the like is due because the dhimmi can legally own and transfer wine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The act: a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine. The dhimmi is the owner. The ruling: value (qimah) is obligatory on the Muslim.
The ruling is specific to Muslims; for a dhimmi destroying another dhimmi's wine, the like (mithl) is due.
2
Understand the reason for qimah instead of mithl
Because the Muslim is prohibited from owning or transferring wine (mamnu' min tamallukihi wa tamlikihi), he cannot give a like for like. Therefore, the obligation is to pay the monetary value.
This is an exception to the general rule that fungibles (mithliyyat) are compensated by like.
3
Contrast with dhimmi-dhimmi case
If a dhimmi consumes another dhimmi's wine, the like is obligatory because the dhimmi can own wine and thus can provide the like.
This shows that the ruling depends on the legal capacity of the destroyer.
4
Apply to similar cases
Consider if the dhimmi converts after destruction: the passage later discusses this intra-Hanafi dispute. For now, note that the basic ruling is qimah.
The conversion scenario will be covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخمر، فقالوا: نعشرها . قال : لا تفعلوا وولوهم بيعها وخذوا العشر من أثمانها. فلولا أنها متقومة وبيعها جائز لهم لما أمرهم بذلك من غير إنكار فكان إجماعاً. وأورد على هذا الدليل في العناية فقال : لم لا تتركهم وما يدينون في بعض الأمور كإحداث بيعة وكنيسة وكركوب الخيل وحمل السلاح فإنهم يمنعون منها، ولأن الأمر باجتناب الرجس يتناول المسلم فبقي في حق الكافر على ما كان بخلاف الميتة والدم لأن أحداً لا يعتقد تقومهما وبخلاف الربا فإنه مستثنى من عقودهم لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إلا من أربي فليس بيننا وبينه عهد» وبخلاف العبد المرتد يكون للذمي فإنا نقلته لأنا ما ضمنا لهم ترك التعرض لما فيه من الاستحقاق بالدين وبخلاف متروك التسمية عمداً فإذا كان الذابح من المسلمين لأن ولاية السيف والمحاجة ثابتة فيمكن إلزامه فلا يجب على متلفه الضمان وأما إذا أتلف المسلم خمر الذمي تجب عليه قيمته وإن كان مثلياً لأن المسلم ممنوع من تملكه وتمليكه بخلاف الذمي إذا استهلك خمر الذمي حيث يجب عليه مثله لقدرته عليه، ولو أسلم الطالب بعد ما قضى عليه بمثلها فلا شيء له على المطلوب لأن الخمر في حقه ليس بمتقوم فكان بإسلامه مبرأ عما كان في ذمته من وكذا لو أسلما لأن في إسلامهما إسلام الطالب . ولو أسلم المطلوب ثم أسلم الطالب بعده قال أبو يوسف : لا يجب عليه شيء. وقال محمد : يجب عليه قيمة الخمر وهي رواية عن الإمام لأن الإسلام الطارئ بعد تقرر السبب كالإسلام المقارن للسبب وهو لا يمنع وجوب قيمة الخمر على المسلم، ولأبي يوسف أن قبض الخمر المستحق في الذمة فقد تعذر استيفاؤه بسبب الإسلام ولا يمكن إيجاب قيمتها أيضاً لأنه ممنوع منها وصار كما لو كسر قلباً لغيره ثم تلف المكسور في يد صاحبه ليس لصاحبه أن يضمن الكاسر شيئاً لأن شرط تضمين قيمته تمليك المكسور وذلك قد فات ودليله مذكور في المطولات.
Regarding wine, they said: We take a tenth of it. He (the Prophet) said: Do not do so, but rather entrust them with its sale and take a tenth from its price. Were it not considered property and its sale permissible for them, he would not have ordered them to do so without objection, and it was thus a consensus. An objection was raised to this evidence in Al-Inayah: Why not leave them to their religion in some matters, like building a church or synagogue, riding horses, or carrying weapons, for which they are prevented? And because the command to avoid filth applies to Muslims, so it remains for the disbeliever as it was, unlike carrion and blood because no one believes them to be property, and unlike riba which is excepted from their contracts due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Except the one who deals in riba, for there is no covenant between us and him,' and unlike an apostate slave belonging to a dhimmi, for we transfer it because we did not guarantee to refrain from interfering with what is due by religion, and unlike the case of intentionally omitting the tasmiyah when the slaughterer is Muslim, because the authority of the sword and argument is established and can be enforced, so no compensation is due for its destruction. As for when a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine, its value is obligatory upon him even if it is a fungible, because the Muslim is prohibited from owning or transferring it, unlike when a dhimmi consumes another dhimmi's wine, where the like is obligatory because he is capable of it. If the claimant (plaintiff) converts to Islam after judgment for the like (wine), there is nothing for him against the defendant, because wine is not property for him, and by his Islam he is absolved from what was in his liability. Similarly if both convert, because their conversion is the conversion of the claimant. If the defendant converts first and then the claimant converts later, Abu Yusuf said: Nothing is obligatory upon him. Muhammad said: The value of the wine is obligatory, and this is a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), because Islam arising after the cause is established is like Islam concurrent with the cause, and it does not prevent the obligation of the value of the wine upon the Muslim. According to Abu Yusuf, the wine was due in the liability, but its recovery became impossible due to Islam, and its value cannot be made obligatory either because he is prohibited from it, and it is like the case of someone who breaks another's pot and then the broken pot is lost in the owner's hand; the owner cannot make the breaker liable for anything because the condition for guaranteeing its value is the transfer of ownership of the broken object, and that is no longer possible. Its evidence is mentioned in the extended works.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage establishes that wine is considered property (muttaqawwim) for dhimmis, based on the Prophet's instruction to let them sell their wine and take jizya from the proceeds. The ruling is supported by consensus (ijma). The commentator then addresses an objection from Al-Inayah comparing wine to other prohibited acts. The key point is that the Quranic prohibition of wine applies only to Muslims, so for dhimmis it remains permissible and valuable. The passage then discusses the guarantee when a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine. Notably, if the dhimmi later converts to Islam, there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Yusuf holds that the guarantee drops because the wine is no longer property for the claimant, while Muhammad (and a narration from Abu Hanifa) holds that the value is still due because the liability was established before conversion. The analogy of a broken pot clarifies Abu Yusuf's reasoning. Students should note the principle that the time of the act determines liability, and that wine in the hands of a Muslim is not property but in the hands of a dhimmi it is.

Key Terms
متقوم (muttaqawwim): Property that is legally valuable and protected, so destruction requires compensation.
مثلي (mithlī): A fungible good for which compensation is normally the like (mithl), not value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim destroys a dhimmi's wine. What must he pay?
A) Nothing, because wine is not property
B) The like (mithl) of the wine
C) The value (qimah) of the wine
D) A fine to the state

Key Takeaway
A Muslim must pay the value (qimah) of a dhimmi's wine because he cannot give the like due to prohibition of ownership.

Effect of Conversion on the Guarantee of Wine
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee after conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the guarantee of wine when the dhimmi owner converts to Islam after the destruction.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf drops the guarantee if recovery becomes impossible; Muhammad holds the liability remains for the value.

This lesson delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute about the impact of the dhimmi's conversion to Islam after his wine has been destroyed by a Muslim. Abu Yusuf argues that the guarantee drops because the wine is no longer property for the converted claimant and cannot be reclaimed. Muhammad, supported by a narration from Abu Hanifa, argues that the liability was fixed at the time of destruction and remains. We will analyze the analogies and reasoning for both positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
Scenario: Muslim destroys dhimmi's wine. The dhimmi then converts to Islam. The claimant (now Muslim) seeks compensation. Abu Yusuf: no compensation. Muhammad: compensation of value due.
The dispute is about whether the obligation survives the change in the owner's status.
2
Understand Muhammad's reasoning
Muhammad: Islam (conversion) after the cause (destruction) is established does not negate liability, just as if the conversion had occurred at the same time as the destruction. The value remains due because the liability was fixed while wine was property.
This is based on the principle that liability attaches at the time of the wrongful act.
3
Understand Abu Yusuf's reasoning
Abu Yusuf: After conversion, the wine (which was owed as value) cannot be possessed lawfully by the now-Muslim claimant. Since the claimant cannot take delivery of wine, and cannot be given value either (because value is for prohibited items only when the thing is still property), the obligation drops. He compares to breaking a pot: if the broken pot is lost in the owner's hand, the owner cannot claim from the breaker because the condition of returning the broken pot is no longer possible.
The analogy: the wine in liability is like the broken pot; once it cannot be returned, the claim falls.
4
Note that Abu Hanifa's position is with Muhammad
The passage says 'and it is a narration from the Imam (Abu Hanifa)' supporting Muhammad's view.
So the majority of the early school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold the guarantee remains.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Ruling on Destroying a Dhimmi's Pig
Statement of ruling on guarantee for destroying a dhimmi's pig

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on destroying a dhimmi's pig and distinguish between the liability of a Muslim and a dhimmi destroyer.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's pig is property; destroying it requires compensation — value, not like, because a pig has no mithl.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a specific case: what happens when someone destroys a pig belonging to a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Islamic protection). The commentator extends the analogy from wine to pigs, confirming that the pig is considered property (muttaqawwim) for dhimmis, and the destroyer must pay its value. The key distinction is that for wine, a Muslim pays value while a dhimmi pays the like, but for pigs, both pay value because a pig has no mithl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling on wine
Understand that for a dhimmi's wine, if a Muslim destroys it, he pays value (qimah); if a dhimmi destroys it, he pays the like (mithl) because wine is available to him.
This is the established Hanafi position.
2
Recognize the extension to pigs
The commentator extends the same framework to a dhimmi's pig, but notes a crucial difference: for pigs, regardless of whether the destroyer is Muslim or dhimmi, the compensation is the value (qimah), not the like.
The reason is that a pig has no mithl (like) in Islamic law.
3
Understand the rationale for value
A pig is not a fungible good (mithli) because it is not commonly traded or possessed by Muslims. Since a dhimmi's pig is considered property, its destruction obligates compensation, but only in the form of monetary value.
This is consistent with the rule that for non-mithli items, only qimah is due.
4
Locate the supporting transmission
The passage cites al-Tatar Khaniyya as a source for the ruling. The commentator (al-Faqir) remarks that he initially had no transmission, then found one, indicating the importance of textual authority.
This shows the method of expanding rulings through reliable references.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : ولو أتلف موقوذة المجوسي مسلم الصحيح أنه يضمنها . ولم يتعرض الشارح لما يلزمه في إتلاف خنزير الذمي، والظاهر أنه يضمن قيمته كما لو كان شاة كما في موقوذة المجوسي أخذاً من قولهم كذلك الخنزير في حقهم كالشاة في حقنا والتفصيل المتقدم في الإسلام في خمر الذمي يجري في خنزيره وقد قال الفقير : هذا من غير أن يجد نقلاً ثم ظفرت بالنقل. وفي التتارخانية : وإن كان الخمر والخنزير لذمي يجب على متلفهما، سواء كان المتلف مسلماً أو ذمياً، غير أن المتلف إن كان ذمياً فإنه يجب عليه مثل الخمر، وإن كان المتلف مسلماً يجب عليه قيمة الخمر، وفي الخنزير يجب عليهما القيمة لأن الخنزير لا مثل له من جنسه.
And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If a Muslim destroys a Magian's [animal killed by] a blow, the correct ruling is that he guarantees it. And the commentator did not address what is required for destroying a dhimmi's pig, but the apparent ruling is that he guarantees its value, just as if it were a sheep, as in the case of the Magian's [animal killed by a] blow, taking from their statement: 'Similarly, the pig for them is like the sheep for us.' And the earlier detail regarding Islam in the case of a dhimmi's wine applies to his pig. And al-Faqir said: This is without having found a transmission; then I came across the transmission. And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If wine and pig belong to a dhimmi, the one who destroys them is liable, whether the destroyer is Muslim or dhimmi, except that if the destroyer is a dhimmi, the like of the wine is obligatory upon him, and if the destroyer is Muslim, the value of the wine is obligatory upon him. And for the pig, the value is obligatory upon both because the pig has no like of its kind.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage extends the ruling from wine to pigs. The Hanafi school holds that a dhimmi's pig is considered property (muttaqawwim) just like wine, and destroying it requires compensation. The commentator notes that the same distinction applies: if a Muslim destroys it, he pays its value (qimah) because he cannot own the pig; if a dhimmi destroys another dhimmi's pig, he pays the value as well because there is no mithl (like) for a pig. This is consistent with the treatment of wine, but for wine, a dhimmi can pay mithl because wine is available to him. The passage also cites al-Tatar Khaniyya to confirm this ruling.

Key Terms
موقوذة (mawqūdhah): An animal killed by a blow, not proper slaughter, considered carrion.
مثل (mithl): A like item of the same type and quality; used for fungible goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a Muslim destroys a dhimmi's pig?
A) He must pay nothing because a pig is not property.
B) He must pay the value of the pig.
C) He must pay a sheep in kind.
D) He must pay the like (mithl) of the pig.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's pig is considered property, and destroying it requires payment of its value, not its like, because the pig has no mithl.

Ruling on Breaking Defective Items and Spoiling a Woven Mat
Statement of ruling when breaking defective items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking defective items (egg, nut, counterfeit coins) and spoiling a mat, and identify the condition that determines liability.

Core Idea
Destroying a defective item incurs no liability; spoiling a sound item requires restoration or payment of sound value.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq addresses scenarios where someone breaks or damages another’s property that turns out to be defective. Understanding these rulings clarifies the Hanafi principle that liability hinges on the actual value at the time of destruction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage presents three distinct scenarios: breaking an egg or walnut that is rotten inside, breaking coins that turn out counterfeit, and spoiling a woven mat.
Each scenario turns on whether the item had value at the moment of destruction.
2
Determine ruling for defective natural items
For the egg and walnut, the condition is that the inside is spoiled. Since the item had no edible value, there is no guarantee (ḍamān) on the breaker.
3
Determine ruling for counterfeit coins
Similarly, if the coins are counterfeit (sutūqah) and thus have no legal currency value, breaking them incurs no liability.
Counterfeit coins are not considered proper property (māl) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the mat case with two sub-rulings
If the weaving is spoiled, check if it can be restored as before. If yes, order restoration, analogous to a usurped ladder where rungs are separated but can be reattached. If not, the breaker must pay the value of a sound mat.
The ladder analogy illustrates that restorable damage results in an order to fix, not immediate payment.
5
Synthesize the principle
From all cases, the single legal principle is: liability only arises for the actual value at the time of destruction. If the item was worthless (defective or counterfeit), no guarantee. If sound, guarantee is the sound value.
This principle is consistent with the Hanafi maxim that damages are based on value (al-ḍamān bi al-qīmah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتاخانية: أو كسر بيضة أو جوزة فوجد داخلها فاسداً فلا ضمان عليه كذا لو كسر دراهم إنسان ثم ظهر أنها ستوقة فلا ضمان عليه، وإذا أفسد تأليف حصر إنسان فإن أمكن اعادته كما كان أمرناه بها فصار كما لو غصب سلم إنسان وفرق سياهها وإن لم يمكن الإعادة كما كان سلم المنقوض سياهاً وضمن قيمة الحصر صحيحاً.
And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: Or if he breaks an egg or a walnut and finds its inside spoiled, there is no guarantee upon him. Similarly, if he breaks someone's dirhams and then they turn out to be counterfeit, there is no guarantee upon him. And if he spoils the weaving of someone's mat, if it can be restored as it was, we order him to do so, and it becomes as if he had usurped someone's ladder and separated its rungs; and if restoration is not possible, as if the ladder's rungs were broken, he guarantees the value of the mat as sound.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage covers cases where the item destroyed was already defective. If one breaks an egg or nut and finds it rotten, or breaks coins that are counterfeit, there is no liability because the item had no value. However, if he spoils a woven mat, the ruling depends on whether it can be restored. If it can be re-woven as before, the usurper must do so; if not, he must pay the value of a sound mat. The analogy to a usurped ladder illustrates the principle.

Key Terms
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability or guarantee for loss or destruction of property.
ستوقة (sutūqah): Counterfeit or base coins that have no legal currency value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person breaks a walnut and finds it spoiled, what is the ruling?
A) He must pay the value of a sound walnut.
B) He must pay half the value.
C) He has no liability.
D) He must pay the value of a rotten walnut.

Key Takeaway
Liability for destruction of property depends on the item's actual value at the time; defective items and counterfeit coins incur no guarantee.

Compensation for Castration of a Usurped Slave: Abu Hanifa's Khiyar vs Later Scholars
Intra-Hanafi dispute on compensation for castration of a usurped slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Imam Muhammad's ruling on the owner's khiyar when a usurped slave is castrated and explain the later Hanafi objection and the tarjih by al-Sadr al-Shahid.

Core Idea
The owner of a usurped slave has a choice: either claim full value or take the slave as is – compensation for the defect is not automatic.

In cases of ghasp (usurpation), the Hanafi school gives the owner broad rights, including khiyar (option) when the usurped property is damaged. This passage presents an intra-madhhab dispute about the correct remedy when the usurper castrates the slave, reducing his value. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, in the zahir al-riwayah, holds that the owner may either claim the full value at the time of castration or take the slave without compensation. Some later Hanafi scholars opined that the owner should receive only the difference in value. Al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din forcefully rejects this, affirming the preserved earlier position. The lesson illustrates the tension between fidelity to transmitted texts and later ijtihad within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputing positions
Note the triggering act: the usurper castrates the slave, reducing his value. Two positions are presented: Imam Muhammad's original ruling (from the zahir al-riwayah) and the opinion of some later mashayikh.
The value after castration is given as one thousand; the original value is not stated but implied higher.
2
State Imam Muhammad's ruling and its basis
Imam Muhammad gives the owner a khiyar (option): either hold the guarantor liable for the full value of the slave on the day of castration, or take the slave as is with no compensation. The basis is the general principle that the owner may choose between full substitution or taking the damaged property in ghasp cases.
This ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah, the foundational transmission of the Hanafi school.
3
State the later scholars' alternative
Some later mashayikh propose a different calculation: value the slave for work before castration, then after castration, and award the owner the difference. This compensates only the depreciation, not the full value.
This view would change the owner's khiyar to a fixed claim for the decrease.
4
Analyze al-Sadr al-Shahid's critique
Al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din declares that the later view is contrary to what has been preserved (al-mahfuz) from the earlier mashayikh. He asserts that the preserved (and thus authoritative) position is Imam Muhammad's original ruling.
This illustrates the methodology of tarjih: deference to the established transmission (zahir al-riwayah) over later reasoning if it conflicts.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and authority weight
According to al-Sadr al-Shahid, the mufta bihi (operative ruling for fatwa) is Imam Muhammad's position. The later opinion is considered a weak departure, despite being held by some scholars.
The authority weight: Imam Muhammad's ruling belongs to the zahir al-riwayah; the later view is from the nawadir (rare transmissions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خصاه فصار يساوي ألفاً نص محمد أن صاحب الغلام بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمته يوم خصائه وإن شاء أخذ الغلام ولا شيء له. وقال بعض مشايخنا : يقوم الغلام بكم يشتري للعمل قبل الخصاء ويقوم بعد الخصاء فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما قال الصدر الشهيد هشام الدين : وهذا خلاف ما حفظناه من مشايخنا والمحفوظ المتقدم.
He castrated it (the slave) and he became worth one thousand. Imam Muhammad ruled that the owner of the slave has a choice: if he wishes, he can hold the guarantor liable for his value on the day of castration, and if he wishes, he can take the slave and nothing for him. Some of our mashayikh said: The slave is valued at what he would purchase for work before castration, and valued after castration, and he claims the difference between the two. Al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din said: This is contrary to what we have preserved from our mashayikh, and the preserved (position) is the earlier one.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi difference of opinion regarding a usurped slave who is castrated by the usurper. Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) gives the owner an option: either take the value of the slave on the day of castration (before the defect) or take the slave as is with no compensation. However, some later Hanafi scholars (some mashayikh) proposed a different calculation: the owner should be compensated for the decrease in value due to castration. But the prominent later scholar al-Sadr al-Shahid rejects this, affirming that the preserved position of the madhhab is Muhammad's original ruling. This illustrates the tension between direct textual transmission and later ijtihad in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
خصاء (khiṣā’): Castration, an act that causes defect in a slave, reducing value.
خيار (khiyār): The owner's choice to either claim full value or take the property as is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad's ruling, what are the options for the owner of a usurped slave who is castrated?
A) He can only claim the value at the time of castration.
B) He can only take the slave without compensation.
C) He can either claim the full value at the time of castration or take the slave without compensation.
D) He can claim the difference in value before and after castration.

Key Takeaway
The preserved Hanafi position, upheld by al-Sadr al-Shahid, gives the owner a choice: either take the full value at time of damage or take the damaged property with no compensation.

Usurping Wine and Animal Skin: Purification vs. Addition of Value
Ruling on ghasp with analysis of purification vs. addition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the owner's right to reclaim wine made into vinegar and a tanned skin, and explain the legal basis for the difference in compensation.

Core Idea
Purification does not alter ownership; added material value does.

In this lesson, we examine a foundational rule in Hanafi property law: when a usurper transforms the usurped item, the owner's right to reclaim it depends on the nature of the transformation. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq distinguishes between purifying an item (making wine into vinegar) and adding valuable material to it (tanning a skin). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
First, read the general ruling: the owner can take vinegar for free, and take the tanned skin by paying the increase caused by tanning.
Note the different outcomes.
2
Note the difference in compensation
The vinegar is taken without payment; the tanned skin requires payment of the increase in value.
3
Trace the reasoning
Takhleel (making vinegar) is compared to washing a najis garment—it purifies without adding value. Dibagh (tanning) adds valuable material like dye in cloth.
The passage explicitly states: 'التخليل مطهر لها بمنزلة غسل الثوب النجس' and 'بالدفاغ اتصل بالجلد مال متقوم كالصبغ في الثوب'.
4
Apply the method of valuation
For tanning, calculate the increase by comparing the value of the skin before and after tanning. The usurper may detain the skin until payment.
The passage states: 'ينظر إلى قيمة الجلد غير مدبوغ وإلى قيمته مدبوغاً فيضمن ما فضل بينهما'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غصب خمراً من مسلم فخلله أو جلد ميته ودبغ فللمالك أخذهما ورد ما زاد الدباغ فيه يعني يأخذ الخل بغير شيء والجلد المدبوغ يأخذه ويرد عليه ما زاد الدباغ فيه. والمراد بالأول إذا خللها بالنقل من الشمس إلى الظل ومن الظل إلى الشمس، وبالثاني إذا دبغه بماله قيمة كالعفص والقرظ ونحو ذلك . والفرق أن التخليل مطهر لها بمنزلة غسل الثوب النجس فيبقى على ملك المغصوب منه لأن المالية لا تثبت بفعله، وبالدفاغ اتصل بالجلد مال متقوم كالصبغ في الثوب فلهذا يأخذ الخل بغير شيء ويأخذ الجلد ويعطي ما زاد الدباغ فيه وطريق معرفته أن ينظر إلى قيمة الجلد غير مدبوغ وإلى قيمته مدبوغاً فيضمن ما فضل بينهما، وللغاصب أن يحبسه حتى يستوفي حقه كحبس المبيع بالثمن والرهن بالدين والعبد الآبق بالجعل وأطلق في التخليل فشمل ما إذا خللها بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في القدوري : أما لو ألقى فيها ملحاً أو خلل بماله قيمة فعند الإمام يصير الخل ملكاً للغاصب ولا شيء عليه وعلى قولهما إن ألقى فيه الملح فللمالك أخذه ودفع ما زاد فيه . قالوا : أن يعطيه مثل وزن الملح من الخل هكذا ذكروا وكأنهم اعتبروا الملح مائعاً. وإن ألقى فيه الخل فهو بينهما، وإن استهلكه ضمن الخل. وإن غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خلاً فله أن يضمنه مثله إن كان في حينه وقيمته إن كان في غير حينه، ولو أراد رب العصير أن يأخذ القيمة الصحيح أنه ليس له ذلك. وعن الثاني : لو غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خمراً أو لبناً حليباً فصار عنده مخيضاً أو عنباً فصار زبيباً فالمعصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء أخذ ذلك ولا شيء له غيره، وإن شاء ضمنه مثله وسلم إليه وأطلق في الدباغ فشمل ما إذا دبغه بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في الأصل : وإن غصب جلد ميتة ودبغه فإن دبغه بما لا قيمة له فإنه يأخذه مجاناً. وفي الكافي : فإن دبغه بماله قيمة له أخذه وأعطاء ما زاد الدباغ، وأطلق في الجلد فشمل ما إذا أخذه من منزل صاحبه أو أخذه من الطريق بعد ما ألقاه صاحبه فيه لكن قال القدوري : هذا إذا أخذه من منزلة أما إذا ألقى صاحبه الميتة في الطريق وأخذها رجل ودبغها فليس له أن يأخذ الجلد. وفي الذخيرة عن الثاني له أن يأخذ الجلد وإن ألقاه صاحبه في الطريق، ولو كان المدبوغ جلداً مذكى كان له ذلك . قال مشايخنا: لا يفرق بين جلد الميتة وجلد المذكى ذهب إليه الحاكم الشهيد فالجواب في الميتة والمذكاة واحد.
And if he usurps wine from a Muslim and makes it vinegar, or (usurps) the skin of a dead animal and tans it, then the owner has the right to take both and to return the increase caused by the tanning. That is, he takes the vinegar for nothing, and he takes the tanned skin and returns the increase caused by tanning. What is meant by the first (making vinegar) is by moving it from sun to shade and from shade to sun; and by the second (tanning) is with something of value like gallnut and qaraz and the like. The difference is that making vinegar purifies it like washing a najis garment, so it remains the property of the person it was usurped from because value is not established by his action; whereas with tanning, valuable property becomes attached to the skin like dye in a garment, so he takes the vinegar for nothing and takes the skin and gives the increase caused by tanning. The method of knowing it is to look at the value of the skin untanned and its value tanned, and he guarantees the difference between the two. And the usurper may detain it until he recovers his right, like detaining a sold item for the price, or collateral for the debt, or an absconding slave for the reward. And he made the ruling on making vinegar absolute, including whether he did it with something of value or not. But in al-Quduri: As for if he throws salt into it or makes vinegar with something of value, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the vinegar becomes the property of the usurper and nothing is upon him; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if he throws salt into it, the owner may take it and pay what was added. They said: He gives him the equivalent of the weight of salt in vinegar. Thus they mentioned, as if they considered salt a liquid. And if he throws vinegar into it, it is between them. If he consumes it, he guarantees the vinegar. And if he usurps juice and it becomes vinegar in his possession, he may guarantee the like if it is in season, and its value if out of season. And if the owner of the juice wants to take the value, the correct position is that he does not have that. And from the second: If he usurps juice and it becomes wine in his possession, or fresh milk and it becomes buttermilk, or grapes and they become raisins, then the one from whom it was usurped has a choice: if he wishes, he can take that and nothing else, and if he wishes, he can guarantee its like and hand it over (to the usurper). And he made the ruling on tanning absolute, including whether he tanned with something of value or not. But in al-Asl: If he usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it, if he tans it with something of no value, he takes it for free. And in al-Kafi: If he tans it with something of value, he takes it and gives the increase caused by tanning. And he made the ruling on the skin absolute, including whether he took it from the owner's home or took it from the road after the owner threw it there. But al-Quduri said: This is if he took it from his home; but if the owner threw the dead animal on the road and a man took it and tanned it, then he does not have the right to take the skin. And in al-Dhakhira from the second: He has the right to take the skin even if the owner threw it on the road. And if the tanned skin was from a properly slaughtered animal, he has that right. Our mashayikh said: There is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and that of a properly slaughtered animal; this was held by al-Hakim al-Shahid, so the answer for both is the same.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This extensive passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the details of what happens when a Muslim usurps wine from a Muslim and turns it into vinegar (takhleel) or usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it (dibagh). The matn statement gives the general rule: the owner can take the vinegar for free, and take the tanned skin by paying for the increase in value due to tanning. The commentary explains the rationale: making vinegar is merely purification (like washing), so the value is not created by the usurper; tanning adds valuable material (like gallnut) that becomes attached to the skin, so the owner must compensate for that added value. Then the passage delves into an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding when the usurper adds valuable substances to the wine (like salt) during the takhleel process. According to Abu Hanifa, if the usurper adds something valuable, the vinegar becomes his property and the owner gets nothing because the change is considered significant. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the owner can still take the vinegar but must pay the value of the added substance. The passage also discusses cases where the usurped item changes form naturally (e.g., juice becomes vinegar, milk becomes buttermilk) – the owner has a choice. Similar differences exist for tanning: if the usurper uses valuable tanning materials, he must be compensated. There is also a difference regarding whether the skin was taken from the owner's property or from the trash. The commentator notes that later Hanafi authorities (al-Hakim al-Shahid) hold that there is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and a properly slaughtered animal. This passage illustrates the Hanafi attention to detail in property law.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar, which purifies it and changes its legal status.
دباغ (dibāgh): Tanning of a skin, which purifies it and adds value through the tanning materials.
زيادة (ziyādah): Increase in value caused by the usurper's actions or materials.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between making wine into vinegar and tanning a skin?
A) Vinegar is impure; tanned skin is pure.
B) Tanning adds valuable material; making vinegar does not.
C) Vinegar must be given back; tanned skin is broken.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
The owner's right to reclaimed transformed property depends on whether the transformation merely purifies or adds valuable material.

The Dispute Over Making Vinegar with Added Valuable Substance: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ownership after adding valuable substance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the ownership of vinegar when a usurper adds a valuable substance like salt, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
What you add matters: Abu Hanifa says valuable additions transfer ownership; Sahibayn say owner still can reclaim with compensation.

In this lesson, we dive into an internal Hanafi dispute triggered when the usurper adds more than just effort—he adds a valuable substance like salt to the wine to make vinegar. The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to handle cases where value is added to the usurped property.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on adding salt or valuable substance
Focus on the part in al-Quduri: 'أما لو ألقى فيها ملحاً أو خلل بماله قيمة' and the ensuing discussion.
2
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa
According to Abu Hanifa, the vinegar becomes the property of the usurper and nothing is due to the owner.
فَعِنْدَ الْإِمَامِ يَصِيرُ الْخَلُّ مِلْكاً لِلْغَاصِبِ وَلَا شَيْءَ عَلَيْهِ
3
Identify the ruling of Sahibayn
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the owner may take the vinegar but must pay for the added value, e.g., the equivalent weight of salt in vinegar.
وَعَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا إِنْ أَلْقَى فِيهِ الْمِلْحَ فَلِلْمَالِكِ أَخْذُهُ وَدَفْعُ مَا زَادَ فِيهِ
4
Analyze the reason for the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa views the addition of a valuable substance as a fundamental change that transfers ownership; the Sahibayn analogize to tanning where the owner retains a claim albeit with compensation.
The Sahibayn's analogy to tanning is implicit.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Abu Hanifa, the owner gets nothing; under Sahibayn, the owner gets the vinegar minus the value of the additive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غصب خمراً من مسلم فخلله أو جلد ميته ودبغ فللمالك أخذهما ورد ما زاد الدباغ فيه يعني يأخذ الخل بغير شيء والجلد المدبوغ يأخذه ويرد عليه ما زاد الدباغ فيه. والمراد بالأول إذا خللها بالنقل من الشمس إلى الظل ومن الظل إلى الشمس، وبالثاني إذا دبغه بماله قيمة كالعفص والقرظ ونحو ذلك . والفرق أن التخليل مطهر لها بمنزلة غسل الثوب النجس فيبقى على ملك المغصوب منه لأن المالية لا تثبت بفعله، وبالدفاغ اتصل بالجلد مال متقوم كالصبغ في الثوب فلهذا يأخذ الخل بغير شيء ويأخذ الجلد ويعطي ما زاد الدباغ فيه وطريق معرفته أن ينظر إلى قيمة الجلد غير مدبوغ وإلى قيمته مدبوغاً فيضمن ما فضل بينهما، وللغاصب أن يحبسه حتى يستوفي حقه كحبس المبيع بالثمن والرهن بالدين والعبد الآبق بالجعل وأطلق في التخليل فشمل ما إذا خللها بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في القدوري : أما لو ألقى فيها ملحاً أو خلل بماله قيمة فعند الإمام يصير الخل ملكاً للغاصب ولا شيء عليه وعلى قولهما إن ألقى فيه الملح فللمالك أخذه ودفع ما زاد فيه . قالوا : أن يعطيه مثل وزن الملح من الخل هكذا ذكروا وكأنهم اعتبروا الملح مائعاً. وإن ألقى فيه الخل فهو بينهما، وإن استهلكه ضمن الخل. وإن غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خلاً فله أن يضمنه مثله إن كان في حينه وقيمته إن كان في غير حينه، ولو أراد رب العصير أن يأخذ القيمة الصحيح أنه ليس له ذلك. وعن الثاني : لو غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خمراً أو لبناً حليباً فصار عنده مخيضاً أو عنباً فصار زبيباً فالمعصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء أخذ ذلك ولا شيء له غيره، وإن شاء ضمنه مثله وسلم إليه وأطلق في الدباغ فشمل ما إذا دبغه بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في الأصل : وإن غصب جلد ميتة ودبغه فإن دبغه بما لا قيمة له فإنه يأخذه مجاناً. وفي الكافي : فإن دبغه بماله قيمة له أخذه وأعطاء ما زاد الدباغ، وأطلق في الجلد فشمل ما إذا أخذه من منزل صاحبه أو أخذه من الطريق بعد ما ألقاه صاحبه فيه لكن قال القدوري : هذا إذا أخذه من منزلة أما إذا ألقى صاحبه الميتة في الطريق وأخذها رجل ودبغها فليس له أن يأخذ الجلد. وفي الذخيرة عن الثاني له أن يأخذ الجلد وإن ألقاه صاحبه في الطريق، ولو كان المدبوغ جلداً مذكى كان له ذلك . قال مشايخنا: لا يفرق بين جلد الميتة وجلد المذكى ذهب إليه الحاكم الشهيد فالجواب في الميتة والمذكاة واحد.
And if he usurps wine from a Muslim and makes it vinegar, or (usurps) the skin of a dead animal and tans it, then the owner has the right to take both and to return the increase caused by the tanning. That is, he takes the vinegar for nothing, and he takes the tanned skin and returns the increase caused by tanning. But al-Quduri said: This is if he took it from his home; but if the owner threw the dead animal on the road and a man took it and tanned it, then he does not have the right to take the skin. And in al-Dhakhira from the second: He has the right to take the skin even if the owner threw it on the road. And if the tanned skin was from a properly slaughtered animal, he has that right. Our mashayikh said: There is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and that of a properly slaughtered animal; this was held by al-Hakim al-Shahid, so the answer for both is the same.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This extensive passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the details of what happens when a Muslim usurps wine from a Muslim and turns it into vinegar (takhleel) or usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it (dibagh). The matn statement gives the general rule: the owner can take the vinegar for free, and take the tanned skin by paying for the increase in value due to tanning. The commentary explains the rationale: making vinegar is merely purification (like washing), so the value is not created by the usurper; tanning adds valuable material (like gallnut) that becomes attached to the skin, so the owner must compensate for that added value. Then the passage delves into an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding when the usurper adds valuable substances to the wine (like salt) during the takhleel process. According to Abu Hanifa, if the usurper adds something valuable, the vinegar becomes his property and the owner gets nothing because the change is considered significant. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the owner can still take the vinegar but must pay the value of the added substance. The passage also discusses cases where the usurped item changes form naturally (e.g., juice becomes vinegar, milk becomes buttermilk) – the owner has a choice. Similar differences exist for tanning: if the usurper uses valuable tanning materials, he must be compensated. There is also a difference regarding whether the skin was taken from the owner's property or from the trash. The commentator notes that later Hanafi authorities (al-Hakim al-Shahid) hold that there is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and a properly slaughtered animal. This passage illustrates the Hanafi attention to detail in property law.

Key Terms
تخليل (takhleel): The process of turning wine into vinegar, which purifies it and changes its legal status.
دباغ (dibāgh): Tanning of a skin, which purifies it and adds value through the tanning materials.
زيادة (ziyādah): Increase in value caused by the usurper's actions or materials.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what does the owner pay when salt is added to wine to make vinegar?
A) The value of the salt in money.
B) The equivalent weight of salt in vinegar.
C) Nothing.
D) Half the vinegar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, adding a valuable substance during transformation triggers a dispute: ownership may transfer to the usurper or the owner may reclaim with compensation.

Additional Cases in Ghasp: Natural Changes, Location of Skin, and Equalizing Dead and Slaughtered Skin
Additional rulings on natural transformation and location disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the owner's options when usurped property changes naturally (juice to vinegar, milk to buttermilk, grapes to raisins) and state the ruling on reclaiming tanned skin taken from the road, as well as the view of al-Hakim al-Shahid on dead vs. slaughtered skin.

Core Idea
Natural changes give the owner a choice; location and origin of skin affect reclaim rights.

The passage also addresses cases where the usurped item changes on its own (e.g., juice becomes vinegar) or where the skin was taken from a different place (home vs. road). Additionally, a later authority, al-Hakim al-Shahid, rules that there is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and a properly slaughtered one. These details show the depth of Hanafi jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Read the section on natural changes
Look at the part starting 'وإن غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خلاً' and the following examples (juice to wine, milk to buttermilk, grapes to raisins).
2
Note the owner's choice
The owner has a choice: either take the transformed item as is (and have no further claim), or demand the original's value (like) from the usurper.
فالمعصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء أخذ ذلك ولا شيء له غيره، وإن شاء ضمنه مثله وسلم إليه
3
Read the discussion on the skin's location
Find the dispute between al-Quduri and al-Dhakhira regarding a skin taken from the road after the owner threw it away.
4
Understand the ruling of al-Hakim al-Shahid
The passage concludes by citing al-Hakim al-Shahid that there is no difference between the skin of a dead animal (maytah) and a properly slaughtered animal (mudhakka).
قال مشايخنا: لا يفرق بين جلد الميتة وجلد المذكى ذهب إليه الحاكم الشهيد

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن غصب خمراً من مسلم فخلله أو جلد ميته ودبغ فللمالك أخذهما ورد ما زاد الدباغ فيه يعني يأخذ الخل بغير شيء والجلد المدبوغ يأخذه ويرد عليه ما زاد الدباغ فيه. والمراد بالأول إذا خللها بالنقل من الشمس إلى الظل ومن الظل إلى الشمس، وبالثاني إذا دبغه بماله قيمة كالعفص والقرظ ونحو ذلك . والفرق أن التخليل مطهر لها بمنزلة غسل الثوب النجس فيبقى على ملك المغصوب منه لأن المالية لا تثبت بفعله، وبالدفاغ اتصل بالجلد مال متقوم كالصبغ في الثوب فلهذا يأخذ الخل بغير شيء ويأخذ الجلد ويعطي ما زاد الدباغ فيه وطريق معرفته أن ينظر إلى قيمة الجلد غير مدبوغ وإلى قيمته مدبوغاً فيضمن ما فضل بينهما، وللغاصب أن يحبسه حتى يستوفي حقه كحبس المبيع بالثمن والرهن بالدين والعبد الآبق بالجعل وأطلق في التخليل فشمل ما إذا خللها بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في القدوري : أما لو ألقى فيها ملحاً أو خلل بماله قيمة فعند الإمام يصير الخل ملكاً للغاصب ولا شيء عليه وعلى قولهما إن ألقى فيه الملح فللمالك أخذه ودفع ما زاد فيه . قالوا : أن يعطيه مثل وزن الملح من الخل هكذا ذكروا وكأنهم اعتبروا الملح مائعاً. وإن ألقى فيه الخل فهو بينهما، وإن استهلكه ضمن الخل. وإن غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خلاً فله أن يضمنه مثله إن كان في حينه وقيمته إن كان في غير حينه، ولو أراد رب العصير أن يأخذ القيمة الصحيح أنه ليس له ذلك. وعن الثاني : لو غصب عصيراً فصار عنده خمراً أو لبناً حليباً فصار عنده مخيضاً أو عنباً فصار زبيباً فالمعصوب منه بالخيار إن شاء أخذ ذلك ولا شيء له غيره، وإن شاء ضمنه مثله وسلم إليه وأطلق في الدباغ فشمل ما إذا دبغه بماله قيمة أو لا لكن قال في الأصل : وإن غصب جلد ميتة ودبغه فإن دبغه بما لا قيمة له فإنه يأخذه مجاناً. وفي الكافي : فإن دبغه بماله قيمة له أخذه وأعطاء ما زاد الدباغ، وأطلق في الجلد فشمل ما إذا أخذه من منزل صاحبه أو أخذه من الطريق بعد ما ألقاه صاحبه فيه لكن قال القدوري : هذا إذا أخذه من منزلة أما إذا ألقى صاحبه الميتة في الطريق وأخذها رجل ودبغها فليس له أن يأخذ الجلد. وفي الذخيرة عن الثاني له أن يأخذ الجلد وإن ألقاه صاحبه في الطريق، ولو كان المدبوغ جلداً مذكى كان له ذلك . قال مشايخنا: لا يفرق بين جلد الميتة وجلد المذكى ذهب إليه الحاكم الشهيد فالجواب في الميتة والمذكاة واحد.
And if he usurps wine from a Muslim and makes it vinegar, or (usurps) the skin of a dead animal and tans it, then the owner has the right to take both and to return the increase caused by the tanning. That is, he takes the vinegar for nothing, and he takes the tanned skin and returns the increase caused by tanning. What is meant by the first (making vinegar) is by moving it from sun to shade and from shade to sun; and by the second (tanning) is with something of value like gallnut and qaraz and the like. The difference is that making vinegar purifies it like washing a najis garment, so it remains the property of the person it was usurped from because value is not established by his action; whereas with tanning, valuable property becomes attached to the skin like dye in a garment, so he takes the vinegar for nothing and takes the skin and gives the increase caused by tanning. The method of knowing it is to look at the value of the skin untanned and its value tanned, and he guarantees the difference between the two. And the usurper may detain it until he recovers his right, like detaining a sold item for the price, or collateral for the debt, or an absconding slave for the reward. And he made the ruling on making vinegar absolute, including whether he did it with something of value or not. But in al-Quduri: As for if he throws salt into it or makes vinegar with something of value, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the vinegar becomes the property of the usurper and nothing is upon him; and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if he throws salt into it, the owner may take it and pay what was added. They said: He gives him the equivalent of the weight of salt in vinegar. Thus they mentioned, as if they considered salt a liquid. And if he throws vinegar into it, it is between them. If he consumes it, he guarantees the vinegar. And if he usurps juice and it becomes vinegar in his possession, he may guarantee the like if it is in season, and its value if out of season. And if the owner of the juice wants to take the value, the correct position is that he does not have that. And from the second: If he usurps juice and it becomes wine in his possession, or fresh milk and it becomes buttermilk, or grapes and they become raisins, then the one from whom it was usurped has a choice: if he wishes, he can take that and nothing else, and if he wishes, he can guarantee its like and hand it over (to the usurper). And he made the ruling on tanning absolute, including whether he tanned with something of value or not. But in al-Asl: If he usurps the skin of a dead animal and tans it, if he tans it with something of no value, he takes it for free. And in al-Kafi: If he tans it with something of value, he takes it and gives the increase caused by tanning. And he made the ruling on the skin absolute, including whether he took it from the owner's home or took it from the road after the owner threw it there. But al-Quduri said: This is if he took it from his home; but if the owner threw the dead animal on the road and a man took it and tanned it, then he does not have the right to take the skin. And in al-Dhakhira from the second: He has the right to take the skin even if the owner threw it on the road. And if the tanned skin was from a properly slaughtered animal, he has that right. Our mashayikh said: There is no difference between the skin of a dead animal and that of a properly slaughtered animal; this was held by al-Hakim al-Shahid, so the answer for both is the same.

Usurping a Slave and Castrating Him: Ruling and Ikhtilaf
Ruling on a usurped slave's castration and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a usurped slave who is castrated and distinguish between the two views within the school.

Core Idea
In castration of a usurped slave, the owner may choose the damaged slave or claim full value on the day of injury.

This lesson examines a specific tort: a person usurps a young male slave and castrates him, altering his value. The owner is given a legal option. Later Hanafi scholars disagreed on how compensation should be calculated, leading to an intra-madhhab dispute. You will learn the authoritative position and the grounds for the difference.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Imam Muhammad's text)
Identify the ruling from the text of Imam Muhammad: the owner has an option to either take the castrated slave for free or claim the full value on the day of castration.
2
Identify the opposing view
Some later Hanafi scholars (ba'dh mashayikhina) proposed that only the difference in value before and after castration should be recovered.
3
Understand the opponent's proof
The opposing view analogizes to other cases of injury where only the actual loss is compensated.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din rejects this as contrary to the preserved teaching from earlier masters; the owner's option is the established ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the first view, the owner can get full value even if the slave still has some use; under the second, only the depreciation is recovered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فخصاه فصار يساوي ألفاً نص محمد أن صاحب الغلام بالخيار إن شاء ضمنه قيمته يوم خصائه وإن شاء أخذ الغلام ولا شيء له. وقال بعض مشايخنا : يقوم الغلام بكم يشتري للعمل قبل الخصاء ويقوم بعد الخصاء فيرجع بفضل ما بينهما قال الصدر الشهيد هشام الدين : وهذا خلاف ما حفظناه من مشايخنا والمحفوظ المتقدم.
Then he castrated him so he became worth a thousand. The text of Muhammad is that the owner of the boy has the option: if he wishes he may claim compensation for his value on the day of his castration, and if he wishes he may take the boy and nothing is owed to him. Some of our scholars said: The boy is valued at how much he would be bought for work before castration, and valued after castration, and he recovers the difference between them. Al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din said: This is contrary to what we have preserved from our scholars, and the preserved view is the first one.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case within the chapter of Ghasp: a person usurps a young male slave (ghulam) and castrates him, causing a change in value. The original owner has an option, as stated in the text of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: either claim compensation for the slave's market value on the day of castration (the day the injury occurred) or take the slave as is without any additional payment. This reflects the principle that the usurper is liable for any diminution in value caused by his act. Some later Hanafi scholars (some of our scholars) proposed an alternative method: determine the slave's value for work before castration and compare it to his value after castration, and the usurper would pay only the difference. However, al-Sadr al-Shahid Hisham al-Din rejects this as contrary to the established teaching of the earlier Hanafi masters (mashayikh). The preserved view (al-mahfuzh al-mutaqaddim) is the first one: the owner's option between taking the slave for free or claiming full value on the day of injury. This ikhtilaf illustrates that later jurists sometimes introduced alternative calculations, but the authoritative position remains the original text of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms
خصاء (khusa'): Castration of a male slave. The text discusses its effect on the slave's value and the owner's rights.
بالخيار (bil-khiyar): Having an option or choice between two alternatives. The owner may choose to take the slave or claim value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text of Muhammad, what option does the owner of a usurped and castrated slave have?
A) The owner must take the castrated slave and nothing else.
B) The owner may claim compensation for the slave's value on the day of castration or take the slave for free.
C) The owner may only claim the difference between the slave's value before and after castration.
D) The usurper must pay the full value but the slave remains with the usurper.

Key Takeaway
The owner of a usurped and castrated slave may either take the slave for free or claim its full value on the day of castration; this is the mufta bihi view.
