The Three Demands in Preemption and Their Procedures
Explaining the three types of demand in preemption and their conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three required demands in Hanafi preemption, explain the conditions and procedures for each, and apply the rules to scenarios involving notification and testification.

Core Idea
Preemption in Hanafi law requires three sequential demands; failure to make any one voids the right.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains the procedural framework for claiming the right of preemption (shuf'a). Unlike other schools, the Hanafi school mandates a structured sequence of three demands: an immediate demand upon learning of the sale, a confirmatory demand with testimony, and a final demand to take possession. This lesson unpacks each demand, the conditions that trigger the obligation, and the important intra-Hanafi differences regarding who must inform the preemptor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Demands
List the three types of demand: immediate (talab al-muwathaba), confirmation (talab al-taqrir), and taking (talab al-akhdh). Understand the purpose of each.
2
Determine Obligation to Demand
When does the immediate demand become wajib? The text discusses who must inform the preemptor and with what evidence. Note the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
3
Analyze the Manner of Demand
What wording suffices for a valid demand? What expressions void the right? Pay attention to the examples: saying 'Allah' or 'La hawla...' can void.
4
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Compare Abu Hanifa's requirement (two non-just men, etc.) with the Sahibayn's view (one informant suffices). Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) for the preemptor.
5
Apply to Edge Cases
Consider scenarios like inability to testify due to rebels, different cities, or conflicting claims about timing. How do these affect the validity of the demands?

كتاب الشفعة باب طلب الشفعة المشتري أو عند العقار ثم لا تسقط بالتأخير فإن طلب عند القاضي سأل المدعى عليه. محمد أن له التأمل إلى آخر المجلس كالمخير لأنه تملك ولا بد من التأمل وهو اختيار الكرخي. وبعض المشايخ وفي التجريد وهو أصح الروايتين. وفي الفتاوى العتابية: ولو سكت مكرهاً لا يبطل. وكيفية الطلب على الصحيح أن يكون بلفظ الماضي أو المستقبل إذا كان لفظه يفهم منه طلب الشفعة وفي الظهيرية : شفعته لي كان ذلك طلباً. ومن الناس من لو قال طلبت وأخذت بطلت شفعته لأن كلامه وقع في الابتداء والصحيح أنه لا يبطل لأنه أنشأ عرفاً. ولو قال بعد ما بلغه الخبر الله أو لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم أو سبحان الله تبطل شفعته على ما اختاره الكرخي لأن الأول حمد على الخلاص، والثاني تعجب، والثالث لافتتاح الكلام، ولا يدل شيء منها على الإعراض. وكذا إذا قال من ابتاعها أو بكم بيعت لأنه يرغب فيها بثمن دون ثمن، وكذا لو قال خلص الله ولا يجب الطلب حتى يخبره رجلان غير عدلين أو واحد عدل أو رجل وامرأتان لأنه فيه التزام من وجه فيشترط له أحد شطري الشهادة؛ هذا قول الإمام وعندهما يجب عليه الطلب إذا أخبره واحد حراً كان أو عبداً، صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، إذا كان الخبر حقاً، ولو أخبر المشتري بنفسه يجب عليه الطلب بالإجماع كيفما كان لأنه خصم والعدد والعدالة لا تعتبر في الخصم. قال رحمه الله : (ثم على البائع لو في يده أو على المشتري أو عند العقار) وهذا طلب التقرير وفيه طلب ثالث وهو طلب الأخذ ولا بد من هذه الثلاثة، ولا بد من الإشهاد في هذا لأنه يحتاج إليه لأثباته عند القاضي كما تقرر. ولا يمكنه الإشهاد على طلب المواثبة ظاهراً حتى لو أمكنه ذلك وأشهد عند طلب المواثبة بأن بلغه بحضرة الشهود والمشتري والبائع حاضر وكان ذلك عند العقار يكفيه ويقوم ذلك مقام الطلبين ؛ ذكره شيخ الإسلام وفي العناية : ولو باع إلى أجل فاسد فعجل المشتري الثمن جاز البيع وثبتت الشفعة وكذا إذا باع الأرض وفيها زرع وفي الخيار المؤبد والأجل إلى القطاف جاز أخذه بالشفعة فإن لم يطلب بطلت . وإذا اشترى رجل من أهل البغي داراً من رجل في عسكر أهل العدل فإن كان لا يقدر أن يبعث وكيلاً ولا يدخل بنفسه هو على شفعته ولا يضره ترك طلب الإشهاد وإن كان يقدر على ذلك فلم يطلب طلب المواثبة بطلت شفعته. وكيفية هذا الطلب أن ينهض من المكان الذي سمع فيه ويشهد على البائع إن كان المبيع في يده أو على المشتري أو عند العقار، فإذا فعل ذلك استقرت شفعته. وإنما صح الإشهاد عند هؤلاء الثلاثة لأن المشتري والبائع خصم فيه بالملك باليد، وأما عند العقار فلتعلق الحق به ولا يكون البائع خصماً بعد تسليم المبيع إلى المشتري لعدم الملك واليد فلا يصح الإشهاد عليه بعده؛ هكذا ذكره القدوري والناطفي. وذكر شيخ الإسلام أنه يصح استحساناً. ومدة هذا الطلب مقدرة بالتمكين من الإشهادة مع القدرة على أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة حتى لو تمكن ولم يطلب بطلت شفعته وإن قصد الأبعد من هذه الثلاثة وترك الأقرب فإن كانوا جميعاً في مصره جاز استحساناً لأن نواحي المصر جعلت كناحية واحدة حكماً كأنهم في مكان واحد، ولو كان بعضهم فيه والبعض في مصر آخر أو في الرستاق : وقصد الأبعد وترك الذي في مصره بطلت شفعته قياساً واستحساناً لتباين المكانين حقيقة وحكماً، وإن كان غائباً يطلب طلب المواثبة حين يعلم ثم يعذر في طلب التقدير بقدر المسافة إلى أحد الشفيع هذه الثلاثة . وصورة هذا الطلب أن يقول إن فلاناً اشترى هذه الدار وأنا شفيعها وقد كنت طلبت الشفعة وأطلبها الآن فاشهدوا على ذلك. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يشترط تسمية المبيع وتحديده لأن طلبه غير معلوم لا يصح فإذا لم يبين المطلوب لم تكن المطالبة لها اختصاص بالبيع فلم يكن لها حكم حتى يتبين المطلوب. وأما الثالث وهو طلب الأخذ والتملك فلا بد منه أيضاً لأنه لا يحكم له به بدون طلبه ونبين كيفية هذا الطلب من قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. وفي الهداية : ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح فلو ترك الطلب قبله لم تبطل شفعته . وفي الخانية : لو عجز عن طلب الإشهاد بأن كان البائع أو المشتري في البغاة أو دار الحرب فإن أمكنه أن يوكل بالطلب أو يكتب كتاباً به ولم يفعل بطلت شفعته، فإن لم يمكنه التوكيل والكتاب لا تبطل. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إن كانت شفعته عند القاضي فطلب إلى السلطان الذي يولي القضاة بطلت شفعته وإن كانت شفعته عند الباشاة والسلطان وامتنع القاضي من إحضاره فهو على شفعته وفي النوادر: إذا أراد أن يفتتح الصلاة بجماعة فلم يذهب للطلب بطلت شفعته وفي الأصل: الشفيع إذا علم بالبيع نصف الليل ولم يقدر على الخروج للإشهاد فإن أشهد حين أصبح ،صح، وإن ترك الإشهاد حين أصبح بطلت. اليهودي إذا علم السبت وترك الطلب بطلت شفعته. وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند الشفيع بالجوار إذا خاف يوم أن يطلب الشفعة والقاضي لا يراها فترك الطلب لا تبطل شفعته إذا اتفق البائع والمشتري أن الشفيع علم بالشراء منه أياماً ثم اختلفا بعد ذلك في الطلب فقال الشفيع طلبت منذ علمت وقال المشتري ما طلبت القول قول المشتري. وفي الظهيرية : لو قال المشتري علمت قبل ذلك ولم تطلب فالقول قول الشفيع وفي نوادر أبي يوسف رحمه الله : إذا قال الشفيع طلبت الشفعة حين علمت فالقول قوله ولو قال علمت أمس وطلبت أو كان البيع أمس وطلبتها ذلك الوقت لم يصدق إلا ببينة وهكذا ذكر الخصاف في أدب القاضي حكي عن الشيخ في عبد الواحد الشيباني رضي الله تعالى عنه إنه قال : إذا قال الشفيع علمت بالشراء وطلبت طلب المواثبة لا يقبل بلا بينة منه لكن إذا قال بعد ذلك علمت منذ كذا وطلبت لا يصدق على الطلب. ولو قال ما علمت إلا الساعة يكون كاذباً فالحيلة في ذلك أن يقول الإنسان أخبرني بالشراء ثم يقول الآن أخبرت فيكون صادقاً، وإن أخبر قبل ذلك كما في الصغير إذا بلغت في نصف الليل واختارت نفسها وأرادت أن تشهد على ذلك تقولم حضت الآن ولا
The Book of Preemption, Chapter on Seeking Preemption: The buyer or at the property then it does not lapse by delay. If he seeks before the judge, he asks the defendant. Muhammad said that he may deliberate until the end of the session like one given a choice, because it is acquisition and deliberation is necessary; this is the choice of al-Karkhi. Some mashayikh and in al-Tajrid it is the more correct of the two narrations. In al-Fatawa al-Atabiyyah: If he remains silent under compulsion, it does not become void. The manner of the demand according to the correct view is with the past or future tense if the wording conveys the demand for preemption. In al-Zahiriyyah: 'My preemption right is mine' is a demand. Among people, if he said 'I demanded and took' his preemption is void because his speech occurred at the beginning; correct that it is not void because customarily he initiated. If after the news reached him he said 'Allah' or 'there is no might or power except with Allah the Exalted, the Great' or 'glory be to Allah', his preemption becomes void according to al-Karkhi's choice, because the first is praise for deliverance, the second is amazement, the third is opening speech, none indicate disinterest. Similarly if he said 'who bought it' or 'for how much was it sold' because he desires it at a different price; similarly if he said 'may Allah grant deliverance'. The demand is not obligatory until two non-just men or one just man or one man and two women inform him, because it involves commitment from one angle, so one part of testimony is required; this is the Imam's statement. According to the two companions, the demand becomes obligatory if one person informs him, whether free or slave, young or old, if the news is true. If the buyer himself informs, the demand becomes obligatory by consensus regardless, because he is an adversary and number and justice are not considered in an adversary. He [the author] said: 'Then it is upon the seller if the property is in his possession, or upon the buyer, or at the property.' This is the demand for confirmation, and there is a third demand: the demand for taking. These three are necessary. Testimony (ishhad) is necessary in this because it is needed to prove it before the judge as established. He cannot testify on the immediate demand apparently, unless he is able to do so and testifies at the immediate demand that he was informed in the presence of witnesses with the buyer and seller present and that was at the property, that suffices and stands in place of the two demands; mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam. In al-Inayah: If he sold for an invalid term and the buyer paid the price early, the sale is valid and preemption is established. Similarly if he sold land with crops and the option is perpetual and the term until harvest, it is permissible to take it by preemption; if he does not demand, it becomes void. If a man buys from the rebels a house from a man in the army of the just, then if he is unable to send an agent nor enter himself, his preemption remains and it does not harm him to omit testification. If he is able to do that and did not make the immediate demand, his preemption is void. The manner of this demand is that he rises from the place where he heard and testifies against the seller if the sold item is in his hand, or against the buyer, or at the property. When he does that, his preemption is established. Testimony is valid against these three because the buyer and seller are adversaries regarding ownership by possession; regarding the property, due to the right being attached to it. The seller is not an adversary after delivery of the sold item to the buyer due to lack of ownership and possession, so testimony against him afterwards is not valid; thus stated by al-Quduri and al-Natifi. Shaykh al-Islam mentioned that it is valid by istihsan. The duration of this demand is measured by the ability to testify while being able to access one of these three; if he is able and does not demand, his preemption is void. If he intends the farthest of these three and leaves the nearest, if they are all in his city, it is permissible by istihsan because the areas of the city are considered as one area by ruling, as if they are in one place. If some are in it and some in another city or in the countryside, and he intends the farthest and leaves the one in his city, his preemption is void by qiyas and istihsan due to the two places being different in reality and ruling. If he is absent, he makes the immediate demand when he learns, then he is excused in the demand for confirmation according to the distance to one of these three. The form of this demand is to say: 'So-and-so bought this house and I am its preemptor, and I have demanded preemption and I demand it now, so bear witness to that.' Abu Yusuf is reported to require specifying the sold item and its description because his demand is unknown and not valid; if he did not clarify the demanded item, the claim has no specificity to the sale and thus no ruling until the demanded item is clarified. As for the third, which is the demand for taking and ownership, it is also necessary because he is not adjudged for it without his demand. We will explain the manner of this demand shortly, God willing. In al-Hidayah: The demand is conditional upon the expiry of the option; so if he leaves the demand before that, his preemption does not become void. In al-Khaniyyah: If he is unable to make the demand with testimony because the seller or buyer is among the rebels or in the abode of war, then if he can send an agent or write a letter and does not do so, his preemption is void; if he cannot deputize or write, it is not void. In Fatawa Abi al-Layth: If his preemption is before the judge and he sought from the sultan who appoints judges, his preemption is void; but if his preemption is before the pashas and the sultan and the judge refuses to bring him, he retains his preemption. In al-Nawadir: If he intends to open prayer in congregation and does not go for the demand, his preemption is void. In al-Asl: If the preemptor learns of the sale in the middle of the night and cannot go out for testimony, then if he testifies when morning comes, it is valid; if he leaves the testimony when morning comes, it is void. If a Jew knows on Saturday and leaves the demand, his preemption is void. In Fatawa Ahl Samarqand: The preemptor by adjacency, if he fears for his life if he demands preemption and the judge does not see it, then he leaves the demand, his preemption does not become void. If the seller and buyer agree that the preemptor knew of the purchase some days ago, then they differ about the demand: the preemptor says 'I demanded since I knew' and the buyer says 'you did not demand', the statement is the buyer's. In al-Zahiriyyah: If the buyer says 'you knew before and did not demand', the statement is the preemptor's. In Nawadir Abi Yusuf: If the preemptor says 'I demanded preemption when I knew', his statement is accepted; if he says 'I knew yesterday and demanded' or 'the sale was yesterday and I demanded at that time', he is not believed except with evidence. Thus al-Khassaf mentioned in Adab al-Qadi. Narrated from al-Shaykh 'Abd al-Wahid al-Shaybani: If the preemptor says 'I knew of the purchase and made the immediate demand', it is not accepted without evidence; but if he later says 'I knew since such time and demanded', he is not believed regarding the demand. If he says 'I only knew now', he is lying. The legal stratagem is that one says 'someone informed me of the purchase' then says 'now I was informed', so he is truthful, even if he was informed before. As in the case of a minor who reaches puberty in the middle of the night and chooses herself and wants to testify to that, she says 'I menstruated now' and nothing...

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the chapter on seeking preemption (shuf'a). The author details the three sequential demands required for a preemptor to secure his right: the immediate demand (talab al-muwathaba) upon learning of the sale, the demand for confirmation (talab al-taqrir) by testifying against the seller, buyer, or at the property, and the demand for taking possession (talab al-akhdh). The passage begins by stating that preemption is not lost by mere delay; if the preemptor goes to the judge, the judge will ask the defendant. Imam Muhammad allows deliberation until the end of the session, akin to a choice, because it is an acquisition requiring thought. This is the preferred view of al-Karkhi and considered the more correct of two narrations. The correct manner of the demand is using past or future tense that clearly requests preemption; saying 'my preemption right is mine' suffices. Saying 'I demanded and took' does not void it because customarily it is an initiation. However, certain utterances like praising Allah for deliverance ('Allah'), expressing amazement ('La hawla...'), or saying 'Subhan Allah' void the preemption according to al-Karkhi, as they indicate disinterest. Similarly, asking the price or saying 'may Allah deliver' shows disinterest. Regarding notification, Imam Abu Hanifa requires two non-just men or one just man or a man and two women to inform the preemptor before the demand becomes obligatory, because it entails a commitment akin to testimony. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that one informant suffices, regardless of status. If the buyer himself informs, it is obligatory by consensus. The second demand (talab al-taqrir) requires testifying against the seller (if still in possession), the buyer, or at the property. Immediate testimony at the property when informed can substitute for the two demands. The duration of this demand is the time it takes to find the opportunity to testify; if the preemptor delays carelessly, preemption is lost. If the parties are in different cities, the preemptor must choose the closest. Absentees are excused for the distance. Abu Yusuf insists on specifying the property in the demand; otherwise it is invalid. The third demand (talab al-akhdh) is necessary because the judge cannot award without it. The passage further discusses various scenarios: invalidity of option periods, inability to testify due to rebels or dar al-harb (requiring deputization or writing), and conflicting claims about notification and demand. The preemptor's statement about demand is generally accepted without evidence if timely, but if delayed, evidence is needed. A legal stratagem allows someone who was informed earlier to say 'now I was informed' to avoid lying. Overall, the passage presents the Hanafi procedural framework for preemption, incorporating multiple authoritative opinions and practical exceptions.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathaba): The immediate demand that the preemptor must make upon learning of the sale, without delay.
طلب التقرير (talab al-taqrir): The second demand, where the preemptor testifies (yushhid) against the seller, buyer, or at the property to confirm his right.
طلب الأخذ (talab al-akhdh): The third demand, where the preemptor formally requests the judge to award him the property.
الإشهاد (al-ishhad): The act of bringing witnesses to testify to the demand of preemption.
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): The right of preemption allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property at the same price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, the immediate demand (talab al-muwathaba) becomes obligatory upon being informed by:
A) Two just witnesses
B) One person, even if a minor or non-Muslim, if the news is true
C) The seller only
D) A judge's notification

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three sequential demands for preemption; the immediate demand is triggered by reliable information, and failure to make any demand in time voids the right.

The Three Types of Demand in Pre-Emption
Defines the three types of demand and their sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three types of demand in shuf'a, explain their sequence and purpose, and identify which type requires witnesses.

Core Idea
Pre-emption requires three sequential demands: immediate claim, formal witnessing, and judicial taking.

This lesson introduces the three types of demand (talab) that a pre-emptor must make to secure his right. Understanding this sequence is crucial because each demand has specific conditions and missing a step can invalidate the right. The passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q details these steps and their proper order.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three demands
List the three types: talab al-muwathaba (immediate), talab al-ishhad (witnessing), talab al-akhdh (taking). Note their sequence.
2
Understand the purpose of each
Immediate demand establishes awareness; witnessing creates legal evidence; taking transfers possession.
3
Note the form of the demand
The pre-emptor must explicitly declare the sale and his claim in front of witnesses.
The example: 'So-and-so bought this house, I am its pre-emptor, I had demanded and now demand, bear witness.'
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf requires naming the sold item; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not stipulate this.
The mufta bihi follows the majority.
5
Apply the rule
Given a scenario, determine which demand is required and whether it is valid.

كتاب الشفعة باب طلب الشفعة
كانوا جميعاً في مصره جاز استحساناً لأن نواحي المصر جعلت كناحية واحدة حكماً كأنهم في مكان واحد، ولو كان بعضهم فيه والبعض في مصر آخر أو في الرستاق : وقصد الأبعد وترك الذي في مصره بطلت شفعته قياساً واستحساناً لتباين المكانين حقيقة وحكماً، وإن كان غائباً يطلب طلب المواثبة حين يعلم ثم يعذر في طلب التقدير بقدر المسافة إلى أحد الشفيع هذه الثلاثة .
وصورة هذا الطلب أن يقول إن فلاناً اشترى هذه الدار وأنا شفيعها وقد كنت طلبت الشفعة وأطلبها الآن فاشهدوا على ذلك. وعن أبي يوسف أنه يشترط تسمية المبيع وتحديده لأن طلبه غير معلوم لا يصح فإذا لم يبين المطلوب لم تكن المطالبة لها اختصاص بالبيع فلم يكن لها حكم حتى يتبين المطلوب. وأما الثالث وهو طلب الأخذ والتملك فلا بد منه أيضاً لأنه لا يحكم له به بدون طلبه ونبين كيفية هذا الطلب من قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. وفي الهداية : ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح فلو ترك الطلب قبله لم تبطل شفعته . وفي الخانية : لو عجز عن طلب الإشهاد بأن كان البائع أو المشتري في البغاة أو دار الحرب فإن أمكنه أن يوكل بالطلب أو يكتب كتاباً به ولم يفعل بطلت شفعته، فإن لم يمكنه التوكيل والكتاب لا تبطل. وفي فتاوى أبي الليث : إن كانت شفعته عند القاضي فطلب إلى السلطان الذي يولي القضاة بطلت شفعته وإن كانت شفعته عند الباشاة والسلطان وامتنع القاضي من إحضاره فهو على شفعته وفي النوادر: إذا أراد أن يفتتح الصلاة بجماعة فلم يذهب للطلب بطلت شفعته وفي الأصل: الشفيع إذا علم بالبيع نصف الليل ولم يقدر على الخروج للإشهاد فإن أشهد حين أصبح ،صح، وإن ترك الإشهاد حين أصبح بطلت. اليهودي إذا علم السبت وترك الطلب بطلت شفعته. وفي فتاوى أهل سمرقند الشفيع بالجوار إذا خاف يوم أن يطلب الشفعة والقاضي لا يراها فترك الطلب لا تبطل شفعته إذا اتفق البائع والمشتري أن الشفيع علم بالشراء منه أياماً ثم اختلفا بعد ذلك في الطلب فقال الشفيع طلبت منذ علمت وقال المشتري ما طلبت القول قول المشتري. وفي الظهيرية : لو قال المشتري علمت قبل ذلك ولم تطلب فالقول قول الشفيع وفي نوادر أبي يوسف رحمه الله : إذا قال الشفيع طلبت الشفعة حين علمت فالقول قوله ولو قال علمت أمس وطلبت أو كان البيع أمس وطلبتها ذلك الوقت لم يصدق إلا ببينة وهكذا ذكر الخصاف في أدب القاضي حكي عن الشيخ في عبد الواحد الشيباني رضي الله تعالى عنه إنه قال : إذا قال الشفيع علمت بالشراء وطلبت طلب المواثبة لا يقبل بلا بينة منه لكن إذا قال بعد ذلك علمت منذ كذا وطلبت لا يصدق على الطلب. ولو قال ما علمت إلا الساعة يكون كاذباً فالحيلة في ذلك أن يقول الإنسان أخبرني بالشراء ثم يقول الآن أخبرت فيكون صادقاً، وإن أخبر قبل ذلك كما في الصغير إذا بلغت في نصف الليل واختارت نفسها وأرادت أن تشهد على ذلك تقول حضت الآن ولا تقول حضت نصف الليل واخترت نفسي فإنها لا تصدق في اختيارها نفسها ولكن تقول على نحو ما سبق وتكون صادقة في قولها الآن حضت .
Book of Pre-emption, Chapter of Demand for Pre-emption. If all of them are in his city, it is permissible by istihsan because the districts of the city are treated as one district legally, as if they are in one place. If some of them are in it and some in another city or in the countryside, and he aimed for the farthest and left the one in his city, his pre-emption is void both by qiyas and istihsan due to the difference of the two places in reality and law. If he is absent, he makes the immediate demand when he learns, then he is excused for the demand of appraisal up to the distance to one of the pre-emptors. These are the three. The form of this demand is that he says: 'So-and-so bought this house and I am its pre-emptor, and I had demanded pre-emption and I demand it now, so bear witness to that.' From Abu Yusuf: he stipulates naming the sold item and specifying it because his demand is not known, so it is not valid. So if he does not clarify the demanded item, the claim has no connection to the sale, so it has no ruling until the demanded item is clarified. As for the third, which is the demand of taking and ownership, it is also necessary because the judge does not rule for him without his demand, and we will explain the manner of this demand shortly, God willing. In al-Hidayah: And the demand is stipulated at the dropping of the option in the correct view; if he abandons the demand before it, his pre-emption does not become void. In al-Khaniyya: If he is unable to demand witnesses because the seller or buyer is among the rebels or in dar al-harb, and he could have authorized someone for the demand or written a letter about it and did not do so, his pre-emption is void. If he could not authorize or write, it does not become void. In the Fatawa of Abu al-Layth: If his pre-emption is with the judge, so he appealed to the sultan who appoints judges, his pre-emption is void. If his pre-emption is with the governors and the sultan, and the judge refuses to bring him, he is upon his pre-emption. In al-Nawadir: If he wanted to begin prayer in congregation and did not go for the demand, his pre-emption is void. In al-Asl: If the pre-emptor learns of the sale at midnight and was unable to go out for witnessing, and then he gives witness when morning comes, it is valid; if he abandons the witnessing when morning comes, it becomes void. The Jew: if he learns on Saturday and abandons the demand, his pre-emption is void. In the Fatawa of the people of Samarqand: The neighbor pre-emptor, if he fears that if he demands pre-emption and the judge does not see it, so he abandons the demand, his pre-emption does not become void. If the seller and buyer agree that the pre-emptor knew of the purchase for days, then they differ after that about the demand, the pre-emptor says 'I demanded since I knew' and the buyer says 'You did not demand,' the saying is the saying of the buyer. In al-Dhahiriyya: If the buyer said 'You knew before that and did not demand,' the saying is the saying of the pre-emptor. In the Nawadir of Abu Yusuf: If the pre-emptor says 'I demanded pre-emption when I knew,' his saying is accepted. If he says 'I knew yesterday and demanded' or 'the sale was yesterday and I demanded it at that time,' he is not believed without evidence. Thus al-Khassaf mentioned in Adab al-Qadi. It is narrated from al-Shaykh 'Abd al-Wahid al-Shaybani that he said: If the pre-emptor says 'I knew of the purchase and demanded the immediate demand,' it is not accepted without evidence from him. But if he says after that 'I knew since such and such and demanded,' he is not believed about the demand. If he says 'I only knew just now,' he would be lying. The trick in that is that a person says 'He informed me of the purchase,' then says 'Now I have been informed,' so he is truthful, even if he was informed before. Just as in al-Saghir: If a girl reaches puberty at midnight and chooses herself and wants to bear witness to that, she says 'I menstruated now' and does not say 'I menstruated at midnight and chose myself,' for she is not believed in her choice of herself, but she says in the manner mentioned and is truthful in her statement 'I menstruated now.'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of demand (talab) in pre-emption (shuf'a) and their conditions. The author begins by discussing a scenario where multiple pre-emptors are in different locations: if they are all in the same city, it is permissible by istihsan because all parts of a city are considered one legal unit. But if some are in another city or countryside, and a pre-emptor bypasses the nearer one to seek the farther, his pre-emption is void both by qiyas and istihsan because the places are distinct. The passage then introduces the three demands: (1) immediate demand (talab al-muwathaba) upon learning, (2) demand of witnessing (talab al-ishhad) to establish the claim, and (3) demand of taking (talab al-akhdh) to obtain possession. For the immediate demand, the pre-emptor must declare the sale and his claim in the presence of witnesses. Abu Yusuf additionally requires specifying the sold property. The third demand is necessary for the judge to rule. From al-Hidayah, the demand must be made after the option period expires in the correct view, otherwise earlier demand does not invalidate. Various excuses are discussed: inability to witness due to the other party being rebels or in war territory, and the pre-emptor must still try to delegate or write; otherwise his right is lost. The passage also addresses special cases: if the pre-emptor's only recourse is to the sultan, or if he is delayed by night, or if he is a Jew on Saturday, etc. Finally, it discusses disputes over whether the pre-emptor actually made the demand: the general rule is that if he claims demand upon knowledge, it is accepted without evidence, but if he claims a demand after some delay, he needs evidence. A trick (hila) is provided: the pre-emptor can say 'he informed me' and then 'now I have been informed' to be truthful.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathaba): The immediate demand upon learning of the sale, without delay.
طلب الإشهاد (talab al-ishhad): The demand where the pre-emptor seeks witnesses to testify to his claim.
طلب الأخذ (talab al-akhdh): The demand to take possession of the property after the right is established.
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption to acquire a sold property from the buyer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes talab al-muwathaba?
A) The demand to take possession
B) The immediate declaration upon learning
C) The formal witnessing
D) The judicial petition

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor must perform three specific demands in order: immediate declaration with witnesses, formal witnessing, and petition for possession; failure to do any step can nullify the right.

Conditions and Excuses in the Demand for Pre-Emption
Conditions and excuses for the demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions that validate the demand for pre-emption and apply the rules for excuses such as distance, time constraints, and inability to access witnesses.

Core Idea
Valid excuses (like distance, night, or rebel territory) can allow delay in the demand, but deliberate delay without excuse voids the right.

While the three demands are the core, their validity depends on timing and circumstances. This lesson examines the conditions for a valid demand, including the requirement that it be made after the option period expires, and the various excuses that allow delay. The passage from al-Bahr al-Rai'q details many nuanced scenarios, from absent parties to Jews on Saturday, and even a clever trick for truthful testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing condition
The demand must be made after the option (khiyar) expires according to the correct view.
Al-Hidayah: 'ويشترط الطلب عند سقوط الخيار في الصحيح'
2
Recognize valid excuses
Inability to access witnesses due to night, distance, or the other party being in dar al-harb or rebels (bagha).
3
Apply the rule for alternative means
If the pre-emptor can authorize a representative or write a letter, he must do so; otherwise his right is void.
From al-Khaniyya: if he could delegate but didn't, right is lost.
4
Understand the dispute about evidence
Who bears the burden of proving the demand? The passage gives rules from various authorities: in some cases the pre-emptor's word is accepted, in others he needs evidence.
5
Analyze the hila (legal trick)
How can a pre-emptor be truthful when claiming recent knowledge? Compare with the girl's puberty case.
The trick: say 'He informed me' then 'Now I have been informed' to be truthful.

The Jew: if he learns on Saturday and abandons the demand, his pre-emption is void. In the Fatawa of the people of Samarqand: The neighbor pre-emptor, if he fears that if he demands pre-emption and the judge does not see it, so he abandons the demand, his pre-emption does not become void. If the seller and buyer agree that the pre-emptor knew of the purchase for days, then they differ after that about the demand, the pre-emptor says 'I demanded since I knew' and the buyer says 'You did not demand,' the saying is the saying of the buyer. In al-Dhahiriyya: If the buyer said 'You knew before that and did not demand,' the saying is the saying of the pre-emptor. In the Nawadir of Abu Yusuf: If the pre-emptor says 'I demanded pre-emption when I knew,' his saying is accepted. If he says 'I knew yesterday and demanded' or 'the sale was yesterday and I demanded it at that time,' he is not believed without evidence. Thus al-Khassaf mentioned in Adab al-Qadi. It is narrated from al-Shaykh 'Abd al-Wahid al-Shaybani that he said: If the pre-emptor says 'I knew of the purchase and demanded the immediate demand,' it is not accepted without evidence from him. But if he says after that 'I knew since such and such and demanded,' he is not believed about the demand. If he says 'I only knew just now,' he would be lying. The trick in that is that a person says 'He informed me of the purchase,' then says 'Now I have been informed,' so he is truthful, even if he was informed before. Just as in al-Saghir: If a girl reaches puberty at midnight and chooses herself and wants to bear witness to that, she says 'I menstruated now' and does not say 'I menstruated at midnight and chose myself,' for she is not believed in her choice of herself, but she says in the manner mentioned and is truthful in her statement 'I menstruated now.'
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of demand (talab) in pre-emption (shuf'a) and their conditions. The author begins by discussing a scenario where multiple pre-emptors are in different locations: if they are all in the same city, it is permissible by istihsan because all parts of a city are considered one legal unit. But if some are in another city or countryside, and a pre-emptor bypasses the nearer one to seek the farther, his pre-emption is void both by qiyas and istihsan because the places are distinct. The passage then introduces the three demands: (1) immediate demand (talab al-muwathaba) upon learning, (2) demand of witnessing (talab al-ishhad) to establish the claim, and (3) demand of taking (talab al-akhdh) to obtain possession. For the immediate demand, the pre-emptor must declare the sale and his claim in the presence of witnesses. Abu Yusuf additionally requires specifying the sold property. The third demand is necessary for the judge to rule. From al-Hidayah, the demand must be made after the option period expires in the correct view, otherwise earlier demand does not invalidate. Various excuses are discussed: inability to witness due to the other party being rebels or in war territory, and the pre-emptor must still try to delegate or write; otherwise his right is lost. The passage also addresses special cases: if the pre-emptor's only recourse is to the sultan, or if he is delayed by night, or if he is a Jew on Saturday, etc. Finally, it discusses disputes over whether the pre-emptor actually made the demand: the general rule is that if he claims demand upon knowledge, it is accepted without evidence, but if he claims a demand after some delay, he needs evidence. A trick (hila) is provided: the pre-emptor can say 'he informed me' and then 'now I have been informed' to be truthful.

Key Terms
طلب المواثبة (talab al-muwathaba): The immediate demand upon learning of the sale.
طلب الإشهاد (talab al-ishhad): The demand with witnesses.
طلب الأخذ (talab al-akhdh): The demand to take possession.
شفعة (shuf'a): Right of pre-emption.
خيار (khiyar): Option period after sale.
بغاة (baghat): Rebels; those in rebellion against the ruler.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Non-Muslim territory at war with Muslims.
حيلة (hila): A legal trick to achieve a permissible result without lying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a pre-emptor learns of a sale at midnight and cannot go out for witnessing until morning, what is the ruling?
A) His pre-emption is void because he delayed.
B) He must demand immediately even at night.
C) If he gives witness when morning comes, it is valid.
D) He loses the right because he did not go out at night.

Key Takeaway
Timing and excuses are critical: the demand must be made promptly after the option expires, but valid excuses like inability to access witnesses can delay it without invalidating the right.

The Effect of Delay in Paying the Price on Pre-emption
Expands the matn rule on hearing evidence and price delay in pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delay in paying the price in pre-emption and distinguish between delay before and after a judicial decree.

Core Idea
Delay before the judge's decree voids pre-emption; delay after does not.

In pre-emption (shuf'ah), the pre-emptor must bring the price promptly. But what if he delays? The Hanafi school has a nuanced ruling: delay before the judge's decree voids the right, while delay after the decree does not, due to the strength of the judicial decree. This lesson walks through the evidence, the intra-Hanafi dispute, and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the pre-emptor's payment of the price. The ruling is that bringing the price promptly is wajib (obligatory).
The consequence of delay depends on timing.
2
Locate the dispute
Al-Sadr al-Shahid holds that delay does not void pre-emption; al-Hawi holds it does. The fatwa today follows al-Hawi for delay before judgment.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the proof for delay after decree
The text cites ijma (consensus) that delay after the judge's decree does not void pre-emption, because the right is strengthened by the decree.
The ijma is mentioned explicitly: 'لا تبطل بالإجماع'.
4
Distinguish before vs. after decree
Before the decree, the pre-emptor's right is not yet 'set' by judicial authority; after the decree, it is fortified. Hence different rulings.
This distinction is key to understanding the Hanafi position.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
The relied-upon fatwa: delay before judgment voids pre-emption (following al-Hawi); delay after judgment does not (by consensus).
The judge may still detain the pre-emptor until payment but does not nullify the decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في يده ولا تسمع البينة حتى يحضر المشتري فيفسخ البيع بمشهده والعهدة على البائع شفعته. قال الصدر الشهيد والمختار أنها لا تبطل وفي الحاوي : إنها تبطل. وفي جامع الفتاوى الفتوى اليوم على قول الحاوي ا هـ . ثم إذا قضى القاضي بالشفعة قبل إحضار الثمن فللمشتري أن يحبس العقار عنه حتى يدفع الثمن إليه وينفذ القضاء عند القاضي محمد، ولو أخر دفع الثمن بعد ما قال له ادفع لا تبطل بالإجماع لتأكده بالقضاء بخلاف ماذا أخر قبل القضاء بعد الإشهاد عند محمد حيث يبطل لعدم تأكده وفي الجوهرة: فإن طلب تأجيلاً في الثمن يؤجله يوماً أو يومين أو ثلاثاً، فإن سلم وإلا حبسه القاضي حتى يدفع الثمن ولا ينقض القضاء بالشفعة وفي شرح الطحاوي : اختصما إلى القاضي يؤجل الشفيع قدر ما يرى لإحضار الثمن فإن أحضر في المدة قضى له وإلا بطلت شفعته ؛ كذا في الخلاصة.
In his hand, and the evidence is not heard until the buyer attends so that the sale is annulled in his presence, and the responsibility is on the seller for his pre-emption. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said: the chosen view is that it does not become void, and in al-Hawi: it becomes void. In Jami' al-Fatawa, the fatwa today is on the saying of al-Hawi. Then if the judge decrees pre-emption before the price is brought, the buyer may detain the property until he pays the price, and the decree is executed according to Imam Muhammad. And if he delays payment after being told to pay, it does not become void by consensus because it is strengthened by the decree, unlike if he delayed before the decree after the attestation according to Muhammad where it becomes void because it lacks strength. And in al-Jawhara: if he requests a postponement for the price, the judge grants him one, two, or three days; if he pays, otherwise the judge detains him until he pays the price, and the decree of pre-emption is not nullified. And in Sharh al-Tahawi: they disputed before the judge, who gives the pre-emptor as much time as he deems fit to bring the price; if he brings it within the period, the judge decrees for him, otherwise his pre-emption becomes void. Thus in al-Khulasa.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (ijma for delay after judgment). It is an expansion of the matn statement that evidence is not heard until the buyer is present. The text first quotes the matn: 'في يده ولا تسمع البينة... والعهدة على البائع' meaning the pre-emptor (shafi') may sue the seller if the property is still in the seller's possession, and the pre-emptor's evidence is not admissible until the buyer is present so that the sale can be annulled in his presence, and the responsibility for any defects or claims is on the seller. Then the commentary addresses a dispute: does the pre-emption become void if the price is delayed? Al-Sadr al-Shahid says the chosen view is it does not become void, but al-Hawi says it does. The fatwa today follows al-Hawi. If the judge decrees pre-emption before the price is brought, the buyer may detain the property until payment. According to Imam Muhammad, if the pre-emptor delays payment after the judge orders him to pay, the pre-emption does not become void by consensus because it is strengthened by the judicial decree. However, if he delays before the decree, even after giving testimony, it becomes void because it lacks that strength. The text then cites al-Jawhara: if the pre-emptor requests a postponement, the judge may grant one to three days; if he fails to pay, the judge detains him but does not nullify the decree. In Sharh al-Tahawi, the judge sets a time limit based on his discretion; if the price is brought in time, the decree is given, otherwise pre-emption is void. The lesson emphasizes the importance of timely payment and the effect of judicial decree.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption enabling a co-owner or neighbor to purchase the sold property by paying its price.
العهدة (al-'uhdah): The responsibility of the seller towards the buyer for any defects or claims on the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in this lesson, what happens if the pre-emptor delays payment before the judge's decree?
A) Pre-emption becomes void.
B) Pre-emption does not become void.
C) The judge detains the pre-emptor until payment.
D) The buyer loses the right to detain the property.

Key Takeaway
Delay in paying the pre-emption price voids the right only if it occurs before the judge's decree; after the decree, the right is strengthened by the decree and delay does not void it.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Is the Second Buyer a Litigant in Pre-emption?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on litigant status in pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn regarding the litigant status of a subsequent buyer in a pre-emption case and explain the legal reasoning behind each view.

Core Idea
The present buyer is not a litigant because judging him would harm the absent original buyer's rights.

In pre-emption (shuf'a), the pre-emptor must establish his claim against the original buyer. But if that buyer has sold or gifted the property and then disappears, can the pre-emptor sue the current holder? The Hanafi imams differed on this critical procedural question. This lesson explores the reasoning of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the two opposite rulings within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf holds the present second buyer is a litigant; the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold he is not.
Both positions are from the same school—intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
2
Identify the dispute's core
Pinpoint the exact legal question: Can the pre-emptor present evidence against the present holder when the original buyer is absent? This hinges on whether the present holder qualifies as a khasm (litigant).
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
Analyse their argument: judging an absent person (gha'ib) intentionally is impermissible. Making the present holder a litigant would effectively rule against the absent original buyer without his presence, nullifying his rights.
This is based on the Hanafi principle that qada' on a ghayb is invalid.
4
Analyse Abu Yusuf's view
The text does not give his explicit proof, but his view likely considers the present holder as the current possessor/owner. His position allows immediate proceedings.
Abu Yusuf's view is a minority position within the school.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the pre-emptor can proceed immediately against the present holder; under the Sahibayn, he must wait for the absent buyer or produce evidence against him directly.
This directly affects the speed and feasibility of the pre-emption claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي ابن فرشته : باع المشتري الدار أو وهبها من غيره ثم غاب الأول فادعى الشفيع على الحاضر الذي هو المشتري الثاني أو الموهوب له فأنكر الحاضر فأراد الشفيع إقامة البينة قال أبو يوسف: هو خصمه فتقام البينة عليه. وقالا : لا يكون خصماً ولا تقام البينة عليه. لهما أن القضاء على الغائب قصداً لا يجوز وفي جعله خصماً إبطال حق الغائب قصداً فلا يجوز بخلاف ما إذا صدقه لأن الإقرار حجة قاصرة فلا تعدو عن نفسه.
And in Ibn Farishta: If the buyer sold the house or gifted it to someone else, then the first buyer disappeared, and the pre-emptor claimed against the present person who is the second buyer or the donee, and the present person denied, and the pre-emptor wanted to present evidence, Abu Yusuf said: He (the present person) is his opponent, so evidence may be presented against him. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is not an opponent, and evidence may not be presented against him. Their proof: Judging against an absent person intentionally is not permissible, and making him an opponent is an intentional nullification of the absent person's right, so it is not permissible. This is different from if he (the present person) affirmed the claim because an admission is a limited proof and does not go beyond itself.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The scenario: a person buys a property, then sells or gifts it to another, and then the first buyer disappears. The pre-emptor claims his right against the present person (second buyer or donee). The present person denies the claim, and the pre-emptor wants to produce evidence. Abu Yusuf holds that the present person is a valid litigant (khasm), so evidence can be presented against him. The Sahibayn hold that he is not a litigant because doing so would effectively judge the absent party (the first buyer) without his presence, which is not allowed. However, if the present person admits the claim, then admission is binding only on himself, not the absent party. This demonstrates the Hanafi principle that one cannot be made a litigant if it would harm an absent party's rights. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated, but the reasoning of the Sahibayn is given preference by the author's commentary structure.

Key Terms
الخصم (al-khasm): A litigant who is party to a case and against whom evidence can be presented and judgment passed.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): An absent person who is not present in court; judgment against him directly is not permissible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is the present second buyer not considered a litigant?
A) Because he did not purchase the property with consent.
B) Because making him a litigant would effectively judge the absent buyer without his presence.
C) Because the pre-emptor did not bring the price.
D) Because the second buyer is not the original party to the sale.

Key Takeaway
The present second buyer is not a valid litigant because judgment against him would harm the absent original buyer's rights; the mufta bihi view follows the Sahibayn.

Pre-emptor's Right to Sue the Seller: Original Possession as Litigancy
Ruling with comparative distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the pre-emptor can sue the seller but not a depositary or borrower, applying the Hanafi concept of original rightful possession (yad muhiqqa asalah).

Core Idea
Only the original possessor (yad muhiqqa asalah) is a proper litigant in pre-emption; a depositary or borrower is not.

In pre-emption (shuf‘ah), the pre-emptor has the right to reclaim the property from the buyer. But if the seller still holds the property, can the pre-emptor sue the seller directly? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the seller is a valid litigant because of his original possession, unlike a depositary or borrower who lacks such possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify who is allowed to be sued by the pre-emptor. The seller is the proper defendant when the property remains in his hand.
The ruling is permission to sue the seller.
2
Understand the rationale
The seller has 'yad muhiqqa asalah' – original rightful possession. This makes him a direct litigant like the owner.
Original possession is key.
3
Compare with excluded persons
A depositary (muda‘) or borrower (musta‘ir) does not have original possession; their possession is derivative. Therefore, they are not proper litigants.
Derivative possession does not confer litigancy.
4
Derive the general principle
Only holders with original possession (asalah) are litigants in shuf‘ah. This limits the pool of defendants to those directly responsible for the property.
The principle prevents suing mere custodians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وخاصم البائع لو في يده يعني للشفيع أن يخاصم البائع إذا كان المبيع في يده لأن له يداً محقة أصالة فكن خصماً كالمالك بخلاف المودع والمستعير ونحوهما لأن يدهم ليست أصالة فلا يكون خصماً .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And sue the seller if the property is in his hand) meaning the pre-emptor may sue the seller if the sold property is in the seller's hand, because he has a rightful hand originally, so he becomes a litigant like the owner, unlike the depositary and the borrower and the like because their hand is not original, so they are not litigants.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn. It explains that if the seller still possesses the sold property (e.g., has not delivered it to the buyer), the pre-emptor can directly sue the seller. The seller is a valid litigant because his possession is original and rightful (yad muhiqqa asalah). In contrast, a depositary (muda') or borrower (musta'ir) does not have original possession; their possession is derivative, so they are not proper litigants in pre-emption cases. This distinguishes between direct possessors and those holding on behalf of others.

Key Terms
يد محقة أصالة (yad muhiqqa asalah): Original rightful possession; refers to a person who possesses property as its owner or as a direct party to the contract, not merely as a custodian.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following can be sued by the pre-emptor (shafi‘) in a shuf‘ah case if the property is still in the seller's possession?
A) The buyer only.
B) The seller only.
C) The depositary holding the property.
D) Both the seller and the buyer.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor may sue the seller directly when the property remains in the seller's hand, because only original possessors are proper litigants.

The Agent as Litigant Until Delivery
Ruling statement with reasoning and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that an agent for purchase is a litigant in pre-emption until delivery, and explain the analogy with the seller.

Core Idea
The agent is a litigant because he is the contracting party, just like the seller.

In this lesson, we examine the core ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq: an agent appointed to purchase property (wakil bil-shira') is considered a litigant (khasm) for the purposes of pre-emption (shuf'a) as long as he has not delivered the property to his principal. The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the right to litigate arises from the contract itself, and the agent is the direct party to the contract. The commentary draws an analogy with the seller, who similarly is a litigant only until delivery. This lesson will break down the ruling, its conditions, and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the ruling from the matn: the agent for purchase is a litigant until delivery to the principal.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
Explain why the agent is a litigant: because the right to litigate belongs to the contracting party, and the agent is the one who contracted.
This is based on the principle that litigation rights follow the contract.
3
Analyze the Analogy with the Seller
The text compares the agent to a seller: both are litigants only while they have possession and ownership. Once delivery occurs, they lose that status.
The analogy strengthens the ruling by showing consistency.
4
Identify the Condition
The condition for being a litigant is non-delivery. Once delivery occurs, the agent is no longer a litigant.
Delivery transfers possession and ownership to the principal.
5
Note the Exception for Principal’s Presence
The principal’s presence is not required for judgment because the agent represents him. This also applies to fathers and guardians.
This simplifies litigation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والوكيل بالشراء خصم ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل) لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد وهي إلى العاقد أصيلاً كان أو وكيلاً ولهذا لو كان البائع وكيلاً كان للشفيع أن يخاصمه ويأخذها منه بحضور المشتري كما إذا كان البائع هو المالك إلا أنه إذا سلمها إلى الموكل لا يد للوكيل ولا ملك له ولا يكون خصماً بعده فصار كالبائع فإنه يكون خصماً ما لم يسلمه إلى المشتري، فإذا سلمها إليه ما يبق له يد ولا ملك فيخرج من أن يكون خصماً غير أنه لا يشترط للقضاء حضور الموكل لأن الوكيل نائب عنه والأب والوصي كالوكيل. وظاهر العبارة أنه خصم ما لم يسلم أقام البينة على الوكالة أو لا، أشهد أنه اشتراها لفلان أو لا. وفي جامع الفتاوى عن الثاني فيمن اشترى داراً فقال عند عقد البيع اشتريتها لفلان وأشهد عن ذلك ثم جاء الشفيع يطلبها فهو خصم إلا أن يقيم البينة أن فلاناً وكله فحينئذ لا يكون خصماً. وفي الأصل : إذا قال المشتري قبل أن يخاصم في الشفعة اشتريت هذه لفلان وسلمها إليه ثم حضر الشفيع فلا خصومة بينهما، ولو أقر بذلك بعد ما خاصمه لم يقبل منه، ولو أقام البينة لم تقبل وفي المنتقى مثل ما في جامع الفتاوى . وفي السراجية : وكيل باع داراً وقبضها المشتري فوكل الشفيع البائع في في لم يصح . وفي : إذا كان البائع وكيل الغائب فللشفيع أخذها منه إذا كانت في يده، ولو سلمها إلى الكافي : داره فباعها بألف وفي فتاوى سمرقند : إذا وكل رجلاً . الموكل لا يطلب ولا يأخذها منه . ذلك الأمر فليس للشفيع أن يأخذها بالشفعة إلا درهم ثم . حط المشتري درهم وضمن بألف ا هـ. وفي التتارخانية : لو اشترى لغيره بغير أمره فهو خصم ما لم يسلم العين لمن اشتراها له فلو قال المؤلف والمشتري لغيره خصم ما لم يسلم لكان أولى لأنه يشمل الفضولي والأب والوصي ويفيد أن الوكالة ليست بقيد .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the agent for purchase is a litigant as long as he has not delivered to the principal). Because the right of litigation in it is from the rights of the contract, and it belongs to the contracting party whether he is a principal or an agent. For this reason, if the seller is an agent, the pre-emptor may sue him and take it from him in the presence of the buyer, just as if the seller were the owner. But if he delivers it to the principal, the agent has no hand or ownership and is not a litigant afterwards. So he becomes like the seller, who is a litigant as long as he has not delivered to the buyer. When he delivers, he has no hand or ownership and ceases to be a litigant. However, the presence of the principal is not required for judgment because the agent is his representative, and the father and guardian are like an agent. The apparent meaning of the phrase is that he is a litigant as long as he has not delivered, whether he established evidence of agency or not, whether he testified that he bought it for so-and-so or not. And in Jami' al-Fatawa from the second: In the case of one who bought a house and said at the time of the contract 'I bought it for so-and-so' and testified to that, then the pre-emptor came to claim it, he is a litigant unless he proves that so-and-so had appointed him as agent; then he is not a litigant. In al-Asl: If the buyer, before being sued in pre-emption, said 'I bought this for so-and-so' and delivered it to him, then the pre-emptor came, there is no litigation between them. If he admitted that after being sued, it is not accepted from him; if he produced evidence, it is not accepted. In al-Muntaqa, it is like in Jami' al-Fatawa. In al-Sirajiyya: If an agent sold a house and the buyer took possession, then the pre-emptor appointed the seller as agent... not valid. And in al-Kafi: If the seller is an agent of an absent person, the pre-emptor may take it from him if it is in his hand; if he delivered it to the principal, he cannot claim or take it from him. In Fatawa Samarqand: If a man gave agency... then the pre-emptor cannot take it by pre-emption except a dirham then... the buyer deducted a dirham and guaranteed with a thousand. In al-Tatarkhaniyya: If one bought for someone else without his order, he is a litigant as long as he has not delivered the item to the one for whom he bought it. So if the author said 'and the buyer for another is a litigant as long as he has not delivered', it would be better because it includes the person who buys without agency (fuduli), the father, and guardian, and it indicates that agency is not a condition.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn explaining that an agent who purchases property (wakil bil-shira') is considered a litigant for pre-emption purposes until he delivers the property to his principal. The reason is that the right to litigate arises from the contract itself, and the agent is the direct party to the contract. Thus, the pre-emptor can sue the agent directly. If the agent delivers the property to the principal, he loses possession and ownership, so he is no longer a litigant. This is analogous to the seller, who is a litigant only until he delivers to the buyer. The presence of the principal is not required because the agent represents him. The text then discusses various scenarios: if the agent openly declares he is buying for another, he remains a litigant unless he proves actual agency; if he delivers before being sued, there is no case; if he admits agency after being sued, it is not accepted. The commentary cites multiple sources: Jami' al-Fatawa, al-Asl, al-Muntaqa, al-Sirajiyya, al-Kafi, Fatawa Samarqand, and al-Tatarkhaniyya. The last part suggests a better wording for the matn to include non-agents like the fuduli (unauthorized buyer), father, and guardian.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالشراء (al-wakil bil-shira'): An agent appointed to purchase property; he is the direct contracting party.
الموكل (al-muwakkil): The principal; his presence is not required for litigation against the agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, until when is an agent for purchase considered a litigant?
A) Until the principal confirms the purchase.
B) Until the agent delivers the property to the principal.
C) Until the pre-emptor pays the price.
D) Until the judge passes a decree.

Key Takeaway
The agent for purchase is a litigant in pre-emption until he delivers the property to his principal, based on the principle that the contracting party bears the rights and obligations of the contract.

The Effect of Declaration and Proof on Agent's Litigant Status
Intra-Hanafi dispute on declaration and proof of agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the different positions within the Hanafi school regarding whether an agent's declaration of agency makes him a litigant, and identify the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
An agent remains a litigant unless he proves actual agency, according to the fatwa.

This lesson examines the intra-Hanafi discussion on the effect of the agent's declaration that he bought for another. The apparent meaning of the matn suggests that the agent is a litigant regardless of declaration or proof. However, sources like Jami' al-Fatawa and al-Muntaqa indicate that if the agent proves he was appointed, he ceases to be a litigant. This lesson unpacks the dispute and identifies the operative fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Apparent Meaning of the Matn
The matn says the agent is a litigant until delivery, without qualification. The sharh says this holds whether he establishes evidence of agency or not.
This is the plain reading.
2
Introduce the Dispute from Jami' al-Fatawa
Jami' al-Fatawa reports from Imam Muhammad (the 'second') that if the agent declares at the time of contract that he bought for another, he is a litigant unless he proves actual agency; then he is not a litigant.
This introduces condition of proof to remove litigant status.
3
Contrast with al-Asl
In al-Asl, if the agent delivers before being sued and declares, there is no case. If he admits after being sued, admission is not accepted. This suggests that delivery before suit ends the case, but declaration alone without delivery does not remove litigant status.
Al-Asl seems stricter.
4
Reconcile with the Mufta Bihi
Later fatwa works like Jami' al-Fatawa and al-Muntaqa are relied upon: the agent is a litigant until he proves agency. The mufta bihi is that declaration alone does not remove litigant status; proof is required.
This is the operative ruling.
5
Extend to Non-Agents
The commentary suggests that the ruling should apply to any buyer for another (including fuduli, father, guardian) as long as he has not delivered, because agency is not a condition.
This expands the scope.

Source Text

قال رحمه الله : (والوكيل بالشراء خصم ما لم يسلم إلى الموكل) لأن الخصومة فيه من حقوق العقد وهي إلى العاقد أصيلاً كان أو وكيلاً ولهذا لو كان البائع وكيلاً كان للشفيع أن يخاصمه ويأخذها منه بحضور المشتري كما إذا كان البائع هو المالك إلا أنه إذا سلمها إلى الموكل لا يد للوكيل ولا ملك له ولا يكون خصماً بعده فصار كالبائع فإنه يكون خصماً ما لم يسلمه إلى المشتري، فإذا سلمها إليه ما يبق له يد ولا ملك فيخرج من أن يكون خصماً غير أنه لا يشترط للقضاء حضور الموكل لأن الوكيل نائب عنه والأب والوصي كالوكيل. وظاهر العبارة أنه خصم ما لم يسلم أقام البينة على الوكالة أو لا، أشهد أنه اشتراها لفلان أو لا. وفي جامع الفتاوى عن الثاني فيمن اشترى داراً فقال عند عقد البيع اشتريتها لفلان وأشهد عن ذلك ثم جاء الشفيع يطلبها فهو خصم إلا أن يقيم البينة أن فلاناً وكله فحينئذ لا يكون خصماً. وفي الأصل : إذا قال المشتري قبل أن يخاصم في الشفعة اشتريت هذه لفلان وسلمها إليه ثم حضر الشفيع فلا خصومة بينهما، ولو أقر بذلك بعد ما خاصمه لم يقبل منه، ولو أقام البينة لم تقبل وفي المنتقى مثل ما في جامع الفتاوى . وفي السراجية : وكيل باع داراً وقبضها المشتري فوكل الشفيع البائع في في لم يصح . وفي : إذا كان البائع وكيل الغائب فللشفيع أخذها منه إذا كانت في يده، ولو سلمها إلى الكافي : داره فباعها بألف وفي فتاوى سمرقند : إذا وكل رجلاً . الموكل لا يطلب ولا يأخذها منه . ذلك الأمر فليس للشفيع أن يأخذها بالشفعة إلا درهم ثم . حط المشتري درهم وضمن بألف ا هـ. وفي التتارخانية : لو اشترى لغيره بغير أمره فهو خصم ما لم يسلم العين لمن اشتراها له فلو قال المؤلف والمشتري لغيره خصم ما لم يسلم لكان أولى لأنه يشمل الفضولي والأب والوصي ويفيد أن الوكالة ليست بقيد .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the agent for purchase is a litigant as long as he has not delivered to the principal). Because the right of litigation in it is from the rights of the contract, and it belongs to the contracting party whether he is a principal or an agent. For this reason, if the seller is an agent, the pre-emptor may sue him and take it from him in the presence of the buyer, just as if the seller were the owner. But if he delivers it to the principal, the agent has no hand or ownership and is not a litigant afterwards. So he becomes like the seller, who is a litigant as long as he has not delivered to the buyer. When he delivers, he has no hand or ownership and ceases to be a litigant. However, the presence of the principal is not required for judgment because the agent is his representative, and the father and guardian are like an agent. إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession; in this context, the agent's admission of agency after being sued is not accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When he declares he bought for another.
B) When he delivers the property to the principal.

Key Takeaway
The agent is a litigant until delivery or until he proves actual agency; mere declaration is insufficient.

6 min
Pre-emptor Has Options of Sight and Defect Despite Seller's Waiver
Ruling statement with brief rationale (talil)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the pre-emptor retains khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-'ayb even if the buyer waived them, and apply this ruling to a new scenario.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor is a new buyer — waivers by the original buyer do not bind him.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that the shafi' (pre-emptor) inherits the full rights of a buyer, including the options of sight and defect, regardless of any stipulations made by the original purchaser. The key principle is that the pre-emptor's acquisition is a fresh purchase, not a transfer of the original contract. This protects the pre-emptor from being unfairly bound by concessions he never agreed to.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the pre-emptor's taking of property by shuf'a. The ruling: the pre-emptor has the options of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) and defect (khiyar al-'ayb).
The ruling is stated unconditionally.
2
Understand the rationale (talil)
The text gives the reason: 'because taking by pre-emption is a purchase from the buyer.' This means the pre-emptor's acquisition is legally a new sale between him and the original buyer. Consequently, the pre-emptor is a buyer in his own right and enjoys all buyer's options.
This is a qiyas: pre-emptor is analogous to a buyer.
3
Recognize the consequence of waiver
Since the pre-emptor's rights are independent, any waiver or stipulation of exemption made by the original buyer does not affect the pre-emptor. Even if the buyer explicitly waived the option of sight or defect, the pre-emptor still has those options.
The text says: 'even if the buyer stipulated exemption from them.'
4
Apply to a new scenario
Consider: B buys a house sight unseen, then signs a waiver of all defects. C, a neighbor, exercises pre-emption. C may still inspect the house and if he finds a defect, he can return it to B. The waiver signed by B does not bind C.
The practical difference: pre-emption remains meaningful even if the buyer tried to limit his own liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وللشفيع خيار الرؤية والعيب وإن شرط المشتري البراءة منه لأن الأخذ بالشفعة شراء من المشتري إن كان الأخذ بعد مائة أخذها الشفعة ببيع
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And for the pre-emptor is the option of sight and defect, even if the buyer stipulated exemption from them, because taking by pre-emption is a purchase from the buyer if the taking is after a hundred... taking it by pre-emption by sale.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn. It states that the pre-emptor has the rights of khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight) and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect) even if the buyer had waived them or stipulated exemption. The reason is that the pre-emptor's acquisition is a new purchase from the buyer (according to the view that it is a new contract), so the pre-emptor is entitled to the standard options. The text here is incomplete due to OCR cut-off, but the intended meaning is clear: the pre-emptor's rights are independent of any waivers made by the buyer.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option for the buyer to confirm or cancel the purchase after seeing the property if he bought it sight unseen.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to return the property if a defect is discovered that was not disclosed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does the pre-emptor have the option of defect if the original buyer had waived it?
A) Yes, because the pre-emptor's purchase is independent.
B) No, because the waiver applies to all subsequent owners.
C) Only if the defect was hidden.
D) Only if the pre-emptor did not see the property.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's options of sight and defect are independent of any waivers by the original buyer; he takes as a new purchaser.

Price Determination in Shuf'a Before Seller Receives Payment
Ruling on pre-emptor taking at seller's stated price when seller has not received price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for determining the price in shuf'a when seller and buyer disagree and the seller has not yet received payment, and apply it to cases involving partial reductions, total reductions, and rescission.

Core Idea
In shuf'a, the seller's word on the price is authoritative when the seller has not yet received payment.

When a property is sold and a pre-emptor (shafi') steps in, a dispute may arise between seller and buyer regarding the actual sale price. This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is binding when the seller has not yet received the price. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides clear guidance: the pre-emptor takes the property at the price stated by the seller, unless the price has already been paid to the seller, in which case the buyer's statement prevails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Recognize that the passage addresses a dispute over the sale price in the context of shuf'a, with the seller not having received the price.
If the seller had received the price, the rule would be different (buyer's statement).
2
Apply the rule: seller's statement is authoritative
The pre-emptor takes the property at the price claimed by the seller, because the buyer's ownership derives from the seller's offer.
This is the default rule when the seller has not yet collected payment.
3
Consider exceptions: partial and total reductions
A partial reduction in price becomes apparent in favor of the pre-emptor, but a total reduction or an increase does not.
'Hatt' (reduction): if the seller claims a partial reduction, the pre-emptor benefits; if the buyer claims a total reduction (i.e., the property was given for free), it does not affect the pre-emptor.
4
Handle oaths when seller claims more than buyer
If the seller claims a higher price and the buyer a lower one, they take oaths; whichever refuses, the other's statement prevails.
The pre-emptor takes at the price confirmed by oath.
5
Understand the effect of contract rescission
Even if the judge rescinds the contract between seller and buyer, the pre-emptor's right remains, and he takes at the seller's stated price.
Analogy: return due to defect does not nullify pre-emption rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بما قال البائع وإن كان قبض الثمن أخذها بما قال المشتري وحط البعض يظهر في حق الشفيع لا حط الكل والزيادة وإن اشترى داراً بعرض أو عقار أخذها الشفيع البائع فالشفيع يأخذها به، وإن كان كما قال المشتري يكون حطاً عن المشتري بدعواه الأقل وحط البعض يظهر في حق الشفيع كما بينا، ولأن تملك المشتري بإيجاب البائع فكان القول قوله في مقدار الثمن ما دامت مطالبته باقية فيأخذها الشفيع ولو كان ما ادعاه البائع أكثر مما ادعاه المشتري تحالفا، وأيهما نكل ظهر أن الثمن ما يقوله الآخر فيأخذها الشفيع بذلك، وإن فسخ القاضي العقد بينهما يأخذ الشفيع بما يقوله البائع لأن الفسخ لا يوجب بطلان حق الشفيع ألا ترى أن الدار إذا ردت على البائع بعيب لا يبطل حقه وإن كان الرد بقضاء .
By what the seller says. And if he had received the price, he takes it by what the buyer says. And a partial reduction appears in favor of the pre-emptor, not a total reduction or an increase. And if he bought a house for a commodity or real estate, the pre-emptor takes it [from] the seller, so the pre-emptor takes it by it. And if it is as the buyer said, it becomes a reduction for the buyer by his claim of the lesser, and a partial reduction appears for the pre-emptor as we explained. And because the buyer's ownership is by the seller's offer, so his statement is authoritative regarding the amount of the price as long as his claim remains, so the pre-emptor takes it. And if what the seller claimed is more than what the buyer claimed, they take oaths, and whichever of them refuses, it becomes clear that the price is what the other said, so the pre-emptor takes it accordingly. And if the judge rescinds the contract between them, the pre-emptor takes it by what the seller says, because rescission does not nullify the pre-emptor's right. Do you not see that if the house is returned to the seller due to a defect, his right is not nullified, even if the return is by decree.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage deals with the situation where the seller and buyer disagree on the sale price of a property subject to pre-emption (shuf'a), and the seller has not yet received the price. The author explains that the pre-emptor takes the property at the price stated by the seller, not the buyer. The reasoning is that the buyer's ownership is established by the seller's offer (ijab), so the seller's word is authoritative regarding the price amount as long as his claim is pending. If the seller claims a higher price and the buyer a lower one, they take oaths; if one refuses, the other's statement prevails. Even if the judge rescinds the contract, the pre-emptor's right remains intact, analogous to a return due to defect. This ruling ensures that the pre-emptor's right is based on the original transaction as declared by the seller.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of pre-emption, allowing a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property in lieu of the buyer.
حط (hatt): A reduction in the sale price after the contract, either partial or full.
شفيع (shafi'): The person entitled to pre-emption, i.e., the pre-emptor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When the seller and buyer disagree on the sale price and the seller has not yet received the price, which party's statement determines the price at which the pre-emptor takes the property?
A) The seller's statement
B) The buyer's statement
C) The pre-emptor's choice
D) The judge determines based on evidence

Key Takeaway
In shuf'a, before the seller receives payment, the pre-emptor takes the property at the price claimed by the seller, not the buyer.

intermediate
10 min
Pre-emption Price When Seller Has Received Payment
Ruling that pre-emptor takes at buyer's stated price when seller has received payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption when the seller has received the price, distinguishing between the seller's acknowledgement of receipt before or after stating the price.

Core Idea
Once seller receives price, his word is not taken; pre-emptor takes at buyer's stated price.

In pre-emption (shuf'a), the price at which the property is sold is crucial. If the seller has already received the price, his role in the transaction ends, and the dispute is between the pre-emptor and the buyer. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rule that the buyer's word is authoritative in such cases, and examines the nuances when the seller's receipt is not apparent or the order of his statements differs.

Guided Path
1
Identify seller's status
Determine whether the seller has actually received the price (qabd). If receipt is established, the seller becomes an outsider (ajnabi).
Receipt can be proven by evidence or oath.
2
Determine whose statement is taken
If seller received price, the pre-emptor takes at the price stated by the buyer. If not, the seller's statement may be considered.
3
Analyze order of seller's acknowledgements
If seller first states sale and price, then claims receipt, his initial price is binding. If he first claims receipt then states price, his price statement is rejected.
The right of pre-emption attaches at the moment of the sale acknowledgement.
4
Apply analogy from wills
The same principle applies to an executor (wasi) collecting debts: the order of statements determines acceptance.
See the wasi example in the text.
5
Handle subsidiary disputes
For disputes about building value or demolition, the buyer's statement is generally preferred, with a difference from Imam Muhammad regarding evidence.
